T: Hi, Dongho, how was your weekend?

T: You had fun, oh, good. Did you go anywhere?
S: Yeah, uh, I go, go, went to uncle, uncle’s home.

T: Your uncle has three children?
S: Yeah, uh, one boy and two girls. So three childs.

*T = teacher, S = student

09. 8630 the lesson procedure 300 complete the objectives by filling in each
blank with TWO words. Write your answers 10 the correct order. [2 points]

Students : 2nd year middle school students
Approximate time : 45 minutes
Lesson objectives

Students will be able :

* to describe 3 daily routine using correct verb forms and @ from
a sample paragraph

* to revise writing through @) on first drafts

Lesson Procedure

1. The teacher asks students what they do when they get home every
day.

2. Students take turns asking and answering questions about their daily
routine in pairs. Students take notes on each other’s answers.

3. The teacher provides a sample paragraph, and students choose the
correct expressions.

(As soon as/Since) Taebin finishes school, he goes to tackwondo.
When he arrives, he puts on his workout clothes, and (first/then)
he practices. (After/Before) he finishes, he rides his bike home.
(As soon as/After that), he takes a shower. (After/Next), he eats his
dinner. (Before/When) he finishes dinner, he does his homework.
(Before/While) he goes to bed, he brushes his teeth.

4. Students use their notes to write a short paragraph about their
partner’s daily routine.

5. Students exchange writings and underline their partner’s mistakes
using the checklist.
— Are the present forms 아 verbs used correctly?
— Are the events described in time order?
— Is time order indicated using the expressions focused upon in the

sample paragraph?

6. Students rewrite their paragraph based on Step 5.

02. Read the passage in <A> and the conversation in <B>, and follow the
directions. [3 points]

A

A typical conversation organized around making requests has a common

overarching sequence of interactional moves:

* A greeting exchange

* Preliminary moves toward 3 forthcoming request
* Making the request

* Short negotiation about the request

* Acceptance/Rejection of the request

* Closing/Thanking

B - i - ㅡ
(A low-proficiency English learner asks ber roommate, a native speaker
of English, to 80 buy some bread for ber.)
Jisu : Hi, Kelly.
Kelly : Hi, Jisu.
Jisu : Buy me bread, OK?
Kelly : Do you want bread?
Jisu : Yeah.

## Page 16

Kelly : So, there’s no bread in the fridge? 후

| Jisu :Sorry?
| Kelly : You don't have bread?
Jisu : No.

Kelly : So, do you want me to go to the supermarket and get some
, bread for you?
| Jisu : What was that?

Kelly : Do you want me to get bread for you?

Jisu : Yeah.

Kelly : Do you want it right now?

Jisu : Tomorrow morning.

Kelly : OK. I'll get it for you later tonight.

Jisu : OK. Thank you.

*Jisu = low-proficiency learner, Kelly = native speaker of English

Explain how the conversation in <B> deviates from the sequence of
interactional moves in <A>. Then identify the strategy that Jisu uses when
she does not understand Kelly.

m The conversation in <B> omitted “preliminary moves toward a forthcoming
request” among the sequence 아 interactional move 10 <A>. That is, in <B>
Jisu exchanges a greeting with Kelly and then directly makes the request
of buying some bread for her. During the conversation, also, Jinsu uses a
clarification request when she does not understand what Kelly says.

Mr. Park: The goal of my class is to help students use the language to
communicate and perform authentic tasks. So I want to spend most of my
class time letting students rehearse tasks they need to perform outside the
classroom. I also want my students to have a lot of opportunities to work
together so that they can use their linguistic knowledge to convey meaning

rather than just practice form.

pattern drill activities

role-play based on real-life situations 4 v 〕 v

*1 = poor, 2 = average, 3 = good

Considering the information in <A> and <B>, identify the textbook you would

First, it enables students 10 use language for communication within
real tasks as Mr. Park wishes. Second, group projects provides lots of
opportunities to work together, in which students are encouraged to
convey their own meaning, unlike pattern drill activities.

06. 8630 16 passage in <A> 300 examine the teaching procedure in <B>.
Then follow the directions. [3 points]

A

Processing instruction, a type of focus-on-form instruction, is based
on the assumption that when processing input, L2 learners have
difficulty in attending to form and meaning at the same time due to
working memory limitations. Not surprisingly, they tend to give priority
to meaning and tend not to notice details of form. Processing instruction
uses several principles to explain what learners attend 10 in the input

and why. Below are some of these principles.

The Lexical Preference Principle: In (1), both -es and boy convey
the same information, ‘the third person singular’. Yet, learners prefer to
focus on the lexical item, boy, to arrive at meaning, and often ignore
the grammatical item, -es, while processing the sentence.

(1) The boy studies in the library, not at home.

The First Noun Principle: Learners tend to process the first noun or
pronoun they encounter in a sentence as the agent of action. For example,
they may misinterpret (2) as “Jack collected the data for the project.”

(2) Jack let joe collect the data for the project.

The Event Possibilities Principle: Event possibilities refer to the
likelihood of one noun being the agent of action as opposed to another.
Since it is more likely in the real world that a dog would bite a man
than the other way around, learners would likely misinterpret (3) as “The

dog bit the farmer.”
(8) The dog was bitten by the farmer.

In processing instruction, teachers provide students with structured
input activities, taking into consideration the principles above. In a
structured input activity, students are forced to attend to form in order

to comprehend a sentence.

## Page 19

Guideline for Pre=service Teachers

Teaching Procedure

1. Explicit Explanation
Explain how 3 past tense sentence 15 constructed 10 English. Then
inform students of why they tend not to notice the past tense marker
-ed and thus misinterpret past tense sentences.

2. Structured Input Activity

Have students read six sentences and decide whether they describe

an activity that was done in the past or usually happens in the
present. Then, check the answers together.

(1) They watched television at night.
(2) They watch television at night.
(3) I walk to school on Mondays.

(4) T walked to school on Mondays.
(5) We played soccer on weekends.
(6) We play soccer on weekends.

Identify the principle in <A> that the teaching procedure in <B> focuses
on. Then explain how the structured input activity in <B> helps students
correctly process the target form for meaning.

m The structured input activity in <B> is based on the Lexical Preference
Principle. In this activity, students are exposed to the pairs of sentences
in the activity, in which each pair has the same lexical meaning but
different tenses. Thus, it requires them to focus on the lexical meaning
first and the form (the past tense marker -ed) second. In other words,
through a form-meaning relationship in this activity, they distinguish
the present from the past tense by noticing the past tense marker ‘-ed,’
resulting in naturally processing it.

02. Examine the class observation checklist and notes completed by a middle

school English teacher after observing a colleague’s class, and follow the
directions. [10 points]

Observation Checklist
Instructor: Sumi Kim Unit: 4. Personal Health
Topic: How to treat acne Function: Giving advice
Period: 2/8 Date: Nov. 11
Lesson * have 3 clearly developed lesson plan —-2>-3
Preparation | « prepare interesting multimedia materials - ㅡ 2-<3>
* give clear directions -<2>- 3
Instructional | *156 an appropriate grouping strategy for @23
Strategies group activities
* provide level-appropriate activities -3
Affective
* create 3 warm and accepting atmosphere — 2D

## Page 21

Guideline for Pre=-service Teachers

Notes
* A fun video clip on acne. Ss loved it.
* Twas Kind and patient.
* Group activity (same-ability grouping)
ㅡ Higher-level students did well. Had no problems.
ㅡ Lower-level students had a hard time completing the task, Seemed |

like they needed some felp. ‘/_\l
_ >

In one paragraph, identify one 51009 point 200 one weak point of the
lesson based on the data above. Support each of your choices with details
from both the checklist and the notes. In another paragraph, address the
problems the lower-level students are experiencing by suggesting two
possible solutions and supporting them with your rationales.

m The lesson above reveals one strong point and one weak point:
interesting multimedia materials use(the fun video clip) and an
inappropriate grouping strategy use. The fun video clip motivates
students in class, according to the comment, "Ss loved it" described in
Notes. The use of an inappropriate group strategy, on the other hand, can
be defined as a weak point of this lesson, since lower-level students have
difficulty in completing the task 35 mentioned 10 Notes.

To solve the problems the lower-level students encounter in class,
scaffolding by teachers and mixed level grouping would 66 suggested.
First, Ms. Kim should help them to complete the task by providing
explicit guidelines or linguistic supports. Too a demanding task or a
vague guideline can block successful language learning. In addition,
she might put lower-level and higher-level students in the same group
because the weaker students would benefit from working with students
at a higher linguistic level.

10 behaviorism, language acquisition essentially involves habit formation

n:‘)

[

프
-

;:O

in a process of Stimulus-Response-Reinforcement. Learners respond to the
stimulus (linguistic input), and reinforcement strengthens (habituates) the

response; they imitate and repeat the language that they hear, and when

they are reinforced for that response, learning occurs. The implication is that

“practice makes perfect.”

언 대 한 관 점
언 어 에 대 한 ( ) o chw{wm\ Shrucd ubalism

Behaviorism is the 016017 0131 human beings learn new behaviors through
a stimulus and response cycle. 10 language learning it holds that language is
learned mimicry and memorization 아 form which leads 10 habit formation.
It suggests that the goal of instruction is to replace bad habits(errors) with

good ones(grammatical utterances). This theory resulted in the creation

and extensive use of drills for teaching foreign languages. This method of
teaching relied heavily on the use of memorization of set dialogues and
extensive repetition and drilling. It was developed in the 1950s as a method
for teaching foreign languages to military personnel very quickly. However,
when used 10 the public schools and universities throughout the world, it
fell short of producing competence users of foreign languages. Why is that?

Language production is not based on predictable, set dialogues. Language

use can be unpredictable and it will vary depending on the contexts in which
is produceg';(_sﬁniaheous) (Pagwotics, sewontics )

Audiolingualism
Audiolingualism 행 동 주 의 에 입 각 해 habit formation2t stimulus/response association0fl 의 해

10 the audiolingual method, structures of the target language were
carefully orderedﬁnd dialogues repeated in an attempt to develop correct
habits 야 speaking. Sentences 10 substitution activities, mim-mem (mimic
and memorize), and other drills were usually related only syntactically
(“I go 10 the store,” “You 80 10 the store,” “He goes to the store”),
and they generally had nothing to do with actual events or narrative.

Sometimes, however, these sentences did resemble real communication
in that the situational scenarios to be memorized included greetings
and idiomatic expressions. Rules were presented but often not formally

(interference) of first language (L1) sounds. Listening and speaking skills

took precedence over reading and writing skills. However, in most of the
applications, there was very little use of creative language, and 3 great
deal of attention was paid to correct pronunciation. Often practice sessions

took place in fully equipped language laboratories (Richard-Amato, 2002).

3. 행 동 주 의 에 대 한 비 판

행 동 주 의 에 따 르 면 아 동 들 은 강 화 에 의 해 언 어 를 학 습 하 지 만 , 한 번 도 강 화 를 받 지 못 한
언 어 적 행 동 도 나 타 낸 다 . 따 라 서 어 린 이 의 언 어 는 외 부 적 인 환 경 에 의 해 형 성 되 기 보 다 는
자 신 의 언 어 체 계 를 창 조 적 으 로 발 달 시 켜 가 는 Aolth(Littlewood, 1987).

Imitation and reinforcement have a much smaller role to play in child
language. For example, children often produce forms that they never heard
their parents or other adults say (“I goed” or “two foots”). Thus, imitation
of adult speech cannot completely account for the way children produce

language: “The child’s language is simply too strange” (McLaughlin 1984,
p.15). Furthermore, parents rarely correct their children’s grammatical errors
but respond instead to the message content (Brown and Hanlon 1970;
Brown 1973). If ungrammatical forms are thus positively rewarded (or at
least ignored), how then do children eventually eliminate them? A behaviorist

view of language, which would predict the need for both imitation and_

neganve feedback in the form of overt COrrCC[lOnS, does not seem to explain _

the way in Wh1ch chlldren learn (Hadley, 2001).

Learning takes place in the human organism through a meaningful process
of relating new events or items to already existing cognitive concepts or
propositions handing new items on existing cognitive pegs. Meaningful
learning is the process 야 relating and anchoring new material to relevant

established entities in cognitive structure, As new material enters the

cognitive field, it interacts with and 15 appropriately subsumed under, a
‘more inclusive conceptual system. As 3 result, these associative links create

Using an Advance Organizer, the teacher begins by giving the students a
general orientation to the activities to come. To assist comprehension, this
introduction may be given in the students’ L1. Use of an Advance Organizer
is believed to activate the students’ previously developed knowledge for top-
down processing, and assist them 10 linking the new information to what

they already know.

Constructivism is 3 theory 아 learning that claims that individuals actively
construct knowledge, rather than passively receiving it. They do this by
filtering and organizing their experience so as to match their existing
kno;\;léc.l_geh(arxﬁental representations) of the world. If there is a mismatch
between their e e};perxence and their existing mental representations, they
restructure the latter to accommodate the new information. According to this
view, different individuals who are exposed to the same experiences will

each construct a different and unique reality.

Schematic knowledge

Language learners make use of their knowledge of the world to help them
comprehend texts. Research has shown that learners possess schemata, i.e.
mental structures that organize their knowledge of the world which they

draw on in interpreting what they heard or read.

(2) Individual difference

30

If some people are better at some thins than others — better at analysing,
for example — this would indicate that there are differences 10 the ways
individual brains work. It also suggests that people respond differently to the
‘same stimuli. If we accept that different mtelhgér{ces predon{n;;e 10 different
people, it suggests that the same learning task may not be appropriate for all

of our students.

@)

Personalization

When you personalize language you use it to talk about your knowledge,
e);perience and feelings. Personalization creates better classroom dynamics.
This is because groups are more likely to form and bond if the individuals
in them know more about one another. And the mental and emotional effort

that is involved in finding personal associations with a language item is
likely to make them more memorable. Finally, lessons are likely to be more
interesting, and enhance more motivation rather than the characters in course
books.

(4) Learner—centered instruction

Learner-centered instruction aims to give learners more sayfn areas that
are traditionally considered the domain of the teacher/by providing learners
with greater autonomy. This term applies to curricular as well as to specific
techniques. It can be contrasted with teacher-centered, and has received

various recent interpretations. Learner-centered instruction includes:

@ techniques that focus on or account for learners’ needs, styles, and goals.

@ techniques that give some control to the student (group work or strategy
training, for example).

® curricula that include the consultation and input of students and that do

not presuppose objectives in advance.

@ techniques that allow for student creativity and innovation.
® techniques that enhance 3 student’s sense of competence and self-worth.

Integrated skills activities can provide a model for integrating skills in a

realistic way and is especially useful at a post-elementary level. Another
simple but effective way of ensuring that skills are integrated is to get the

learners to collaborate, in pairs or in groups, on many of the fluency-focused

tasks described. Finally, both simulation and project work provide 3 natural

framework for integrating skills. Project work is described below. First,

however, we need to see why integrated skill activities are important:

@ They provide opportunities for using language naturally, not just

practising it.

® Many pair- and group work activities call for a variety of skills, sometimes
simultaneously, in order to involve all the learners.

® Students seem to learn better when they are engaged on activities which

involve more than one skill.

01. Read the passage and fill in the blank with suitable words. [ 기 입 형 ]

Long followed up his work on organizational pattern in the classroom

with an investigation of the characteristics of small group tasks most

likely to facilitate acquisition. In a study reported in 1981, he found

that two way tasks stimulated significantly more modified interaction

than one way tasks. Similarly, Doughty and Pica (1986) found that

required information exchange tasks generated significantly more

modified interaction than tasks in which the exchange of information

was optional. The term modified interaction refers to those instances

during an interaction when the speaker alters the form in which his or

her language is encoded in order to make it more (1) . , Such

modification may be prompted by lack of comprehension on the part of

the @)

acquirers of language because they associate sounds, words, structures, and
discourse elements with that which is relevant and important in their daily

03. Read the passage and fill in the blank with suitable words. [ 기 입 형 ]

Can acquisition occur simply through exposure to abundant

comprehensible input?

The answer is “NO”. While receiving comprehensible input is a
desired characteristic of second language acquisition, it is not enough.

Acquisition occurs subconsciously 35 3 result of participating in natural

communication where the focus is on meaning. Learning, which refers to

conscious processes that result 10 explicit knowledge about the grammar
properties of a second language. Thus, acquired knowledge serves as

the major source for initiating both the comprehension and production
of utterances. Learnt knowledge is available for use only by the monitor.
Learning cannot lead to acquisition.

(2) 학 습 조 건 (00 내 Hypothesis and Affective Filter Hypothesis)

@ Input Hypothesis
언어 습 득 은 학 습 자 의 현 재 수 준 보 다 한 단 계 높 은 Y% E (comprehensible
1000 7+ 가 제 공 될 경 우 일 어 난 다 . 언 어 적 입 력 자 료 는 학 습 자 의 배 경 지 식, 상 황 ,

According to the Input Hypothesis, the learners improve and progress
along the “natural order” when they receive comprehensible input.
Comprehensible input is defined as second language input just beyond

the learner’s current second language competence, in terms of its syntactic

complexity. If a learner’s current competence is ? then comprehensible
input is i+1, the next step 10 the developmental sequence. Input which is
either too simple (already acquired) or too complex (i+2/3...) will not be
useful for acquisition. Krashen views the Input Hypothesis as central to
his model of second language acquisition.

@ Affective Filter Hypothesis
정 의 적 여 과 막 이란 학 습 자 가 지 닌 학 습 에 대 한 정 의 적 인 변 인 ㅇ
방 향 으 로 작 용 하 는 AL 말 하 며 , 낮 은 \ 동 기 와 낮 은 차 신 감 , 높
있 다 . 따 라 서 성 공 적 인 언어 습 득 을 위 해 서는 이 러 한 정 의 적 여 과 막 이 낮 아 야 한 다 .

The affective filter is “a mental block” that prevents acquires from fully

utilizing the comprehensible input they receive for language acquisition.
A number of affective variables such 35 motivation, self-esteem and
low anxiety can play 3 facilitative role 10 successful second language

acquisition. Low motivation, low self-esteem, and high anxiety, on the
other hand, can work to raise an affective filter. 10 other words, when the

filter is up it hinders language acquisition.

(3) Natural Order Hypothesis

문 법 체 계 는 예 측 가 능 한 순 서 대 로 습 득 되 는 데 , 이 러 한 자 연 적 인 발달 순 서는 문 법
구 조 의 복 잡 성 이 나 의 도 적 인 문 법 지 도 와 는 별 개 로 발 달 한 다 .

The natural order hypothesis states that the acquisition of grammatical

structures 10 3 second language follows 3 predictable natural order. This

orders 16 independent of instructional sequences or even of the complexity 아

the structures to be acquired. Thus, when the learners are engaged in natural

communication tasks they will manifest the standard order.

(4) Monitor Hypothesis

44

모 니 터 란 학 습 자 의 발 화 에 서 오 류 를 감 지 하 여 잘 못 된 표 현 을 개 선 하 거 나 수 정 하 는
장 치 이 다 . Krashenol 따 르 면 학 습 된 언 어 적 지 식 (.680\ knowledge)o| 학 습 자 의
언어 출 력 을 감 시 하 는 모 니 터 역 할 을 수 행 한 다 ,

Learning has only 006 function, and that 185 35 3 monitor or editor to

make changes in the form of our utterance, after it has been produced by

the acquired system. Acquisition initiates the speaker’s utterances and 15
responsible for fluency. Thus, the monitor is thought to alter the output
of the acquired system before or after the utterance is actually written or

spoken.

2. 시 사 점
Krashen suggests that there 316 certain implications for classroom practice if
language instruction is to be consistent with his theory. Among these are:

@ The main function 아 the classroom may be to provide comprehensible input
10 an environment conducive to 3 low affective filter (i.e., high motivation,
low anxiety).

® The classroom is most useful for beginners, who cannot easily utilize the

informal environment for input. That is, it is useful for foreign language

students who do not have input sources outside of class or for those
whose competence is so low that they are unable to understand the
language of the outside world.

® The requirements for optimal input are that it 66 & comprehensible
® interesting and relevant, © not grammatically sequenced, @ provided in

sufficient quantity to supply i+1, and © delivered 10 an environment where
students are “off the defensive.”

@ Error correction should 66 minimal in the classroom; it is of some limited
use when the goal is learning, but of no use when the goal is acquisition.
Error correction raises the affective filter and should, therefore, not be
used in free conversation or when acquisition is likely to take place.

® Students should never be required to produce speech in the second language
unless they are ready to do so. Speaking fluency cannot be taught, but
“emerges” naturally 10 time with enough comprehensible input (Hadley, 2001).

10 second language learning, learning and acquisition can both be

beneficial. Instruction in conscious rule learning and other types of
form-focused instruction can indeed aid in the attainment of successful

communicative competence in a second language.

@ 학 습 자 의 능 동 적 인 참 여 간 과 : 학 습 자 의 노 력 과 전 략 에 따 라 교 사 나 다 른 학 습 자 로 부 터

Input gives learners 11016 credit for eventual success. Certain learners

such as High Input Generators (HIGs) are good at initiating and sustaining

interaction, or generating input from teachers and others. They are likely
to maintain high levels of interaction in the second language and progress
faster than Low Input Generators (LIGs) who interact little.

@ 지 속 적 인 침 묵 기 : 일 정 기 간 동 안 이해 가 능 한 입 력 을 받 았 음 에 도 불 구 하 고 침 묵 기
(silent 26100) 에 머 무 르 는 학 습 자 에 대 한 설 명 을 하 지 못 한 다 ,

According to Krashen, speech will emerge in a context of comprehensible

input after a certain amount of silent period. But speech does not emerge and
the silent period might last forever for some students.

According 10 Cognitive theory, second-language learning, like any other
complex cognitive skill, involves the gradual integration of subskillg/as-

controlled process initially predominate and then become automatic. Thus
the initial stage of learning involve the slow development of skills and the
gradual elimination of errors as the learner attempts to automatize aspects
야 performance. 10 later phases, there is continual restructuring 35 learners

shift their internal representations.

(3) 교 실 수 업 의 예

2012 학 년 도 기 출
An English teacher developed the following procedure for teaching
pronunciation. (Prominent syllables are marked by large-size capital
letters.)

Step 1

a. The teacher writes the following three versions of the sentence I'm
listening on the board.
- I'm LIStening. - I'M listening. — 1 AM listening!

- Further reading

1. Declarative Knowledge & Procedural Knowledge

|

Declarative knowledge 185 concerned with knowledge “about
something” (knowing that). With regard to language, it relates to such
aspects of language as word knowledge (collocation, pronunciation,
meaning). This information is relatively accessible to conscious
awareness. Procedural knowledge relates to cognitive skills (know
how). Using language is thought to involve procedural knowledge
and, unlike declarative knowledge, is relatively inaccessible.

2. Noticing Hypothesis

According to noticing hypothesis, nothing is learned unless it has
been noticed. Noticing does not itself result in acquisition, but it is
the essential starting point. Noticing refers to becoming aware of a

language feature 10 the input.

The non-interface position has been advanced most strongly by
Krashen (1982). Krashen, it will be recalled, identifies two types of
linguistic knowledge in SLA. “Acquisition” occurs automatically when

the learner engages in natural communication where the focus is on

meaning and where there is comprehensible input. “Learning” occurs
35 3 result of formal study where the learner is focused on the formal
properties of the L2. “Acquired” knowledge consists of subconscious
L2 rules which the learner can call upon automatically; “learnt”
knowledge consists of metalingual knowledge which can only be

used to monitor output generated by means of “acquired” knowledge.
Krashen argues that the two knowledge types are entirely separate and
unrelated. 10 particular he disputes the view that “learnt” knowledge is
converted into “acquired” knowledge. This is the non-interface position.
Krashen advances a number of reasons for the separateness of “acquired”
and “learnt” knowledge;

@ There are plenty 아 case 아 “acquisition” where no “learning” has
occurred. These are widely reported in studies of naturalistic SLA.
@ There 316 cases where “learning” has taken place but where it has
failed to become “acquisition”. Krashen refers to the case of “P”
(Krashen and Pon, 1975), who had “learnt” rules like the third person
singular “-s”, but was unable to use them in casual conversation

because she had not yet “acquired” them.

. McLaughlin2} Bialystoke| 모 형 ( 접 촉 적 입 장 , interface position)

@ Even the best “learners” can master only 3 small subset of 116
grammatical rules of the L2. This is because most of the rules are
far too difficult for the average “learner” to follow. Krashen points
out that it often takes a linguist years to describe successfully rules
that are easily “acquired”.

때 문 이 며 , 둘 째 , 간 접 적 으 로는 연 습 을 통 하 여 명 시 적 지 식 을 자 동 화 시 킬 수 있 기 때 문 이 다 .

Bialystok 3180 develops 3 model 야 SLA based on two types of
knowledge which can interact. She calls thes¢ knowledge types
“implicit” and “explicit”, but it is clear from her description of them
that they correspond fairly closely 10 Krashen’s “acquired/learnt”
types. Bialystok suggests that practice serves as the mechanism by
which explicit knowledge turns into implicit knowledge. Thus implicit

knowledge can be built up in two different ways; (1) the primary
means is “unconscious acquisition”, and (2) the secondary means is
through 6 automatizing of explicit knowledge by practice. One
way, then, 10 which “acquired” and “learnt” may be connected 15 in
terms of automaticity. This is a view developed by McLaughlin (1978)
10 his attack on Krashen’s non-interface position. McLaughlin refers
to Schneider and Shriffin’s (1977) distinction between “controlled”
and “automatic” processing. “Controlled” processing requires active
attention, so that only a limited number of features can be controlled
at 3 time without interference occurring. “Automatic” processing
takes place without active control or attention. The important point
is that “automatic” processes are learned foﬂg)_Wing the earlier use of
“controlled processes”. Thus SLA entails going from the controlled

to the automatic mode of operation. It is, therefore, not necessary to
presuppose two unconnected knowledge types such as the “acquired/

learnt” distinction.

[/ Input Processing

Input Processing 이 론 에 따 르 면 하습ː!들은 언 어 적 정 보 의 표 면 적 인 형 태 , 문 법 구 조 보 다 정 보 의 내
용 과 그 의 미 를 먼 저 인 식 하 고 인 지 적 으 로 처 리 한 다 . / message 7 Form)

The input processing theory assumes that learners have preferences for
semantic processing over morphological processing. That is learners process
input for meaning before they process it for form, so that, for example, they
process content words in the input before anything else, prefer to extract
semantic information from lexical items rather than grammatical items (such
as inflections), and prefer to process meaningful morphology rather than non-
meaningful morphology.

10 Long’s (1996) revised version of the Interaction Hypothesis, more

emphasis is placed on the importance of corrective feedback during

interaction. When communication is difficult, interlocutors must “negotiate

for meaning”, and this negotiation is seen as the opportunity for language
development. Merrill Swain (1985) extended this thinking when she
proposed “the comprehensible output hypothesis”. She observed that it is

when learners must produce langmage/fhat their interlocutor can understand

that they are most likely to see the @B of their second language ability
~

and the 0660 10 608 better ways 10 express their meaning. The demands of

producing comprehensible output, she hypothesized, “push” learners ahead

in their develogment.

A key concept 10 Sociocultural Theory is that interaction not only
facilitates language learning but is 3 causative force 10 acquisition; further,
all of learning is seen as essentially a social process which is grounded in
sociocultural settings.

@ Scaffolding Hypothesis

One way in which others help 06 learner 10 language development
within the ZPD is through scaffolding. This includes the vertical
constructions, in which experts commonly provide learners with chunks of
talk that the learners can then use to express concepts which are beyond
their independent means. More generally, the metaphor of scaffolding

refers to verbal guidance which an expert provides to help a learner
perform any specific task, or the verbal collaboration of peers to perform

a task which would be too difficult for any one of them individually.

Collaborative Learning (Scaffolding)

The following constructed dialogue illustrates the negotiation of meaning
in a typical one-to-one communication. In this kind of collaboration, the
“stretching” to higher levels of development become more obvious.

: I throw it-box. (He points 10 a box on the floor.)

: You threw the box.

: No, I threw in 6 box.

 What did you throw 10 the box?

*My... I... paint,..

: Your painting?

: Painting?

: You know ., painting?

: you know. .. painting. (The teacher makes painting movements on an

H H v 역 2 g un 여 <

imaginary paper.)
: Yes, painting.
. You threw your painting in the box.

» g »

: Yes, I threw my painting in box.

The teacher’s input is near the student’s i+1. It provides scaffol

which the student can build. The conversation is about the immediate
environment, the vocabulary is simple, repetitions are frequent, and acting

out is used.

@
‘(c Further reading … —

Swain’s Output Hypothesis : pushed output

Swain preferred to take a stronger stand for the importance of the role
of output in her development of the Output Hypothesis. She argued that
among other functions, output is a significant way to test out hypotheses
about the target language. Swain (1995) suggests that “pushed output”
(i.e., output that is accurate and sociolinguistically appropriate) may 66

Chapter 02 제 2 언 어 습 득 모 형 _ 611

## Page 62

Chapter

4 I:E 제 2 언 어 5528

necessary for learners to achieve higher levels of linguistic and sociolin
guistic competence. According to Swain (1995), pushed output facilitates
acquisitions, as it (1) helps learners to discover that there is a gap between
what they want to say and what they are able to say, (2) provides a way for
learners to try out new rules and modify them accordingly, and (3) helps

learners to actively reflect on what they know about the target language

system. According to Ellis (1997), the use 아 linguistic knowledge under
real conditions of communication. In other words, learners “need to see for
themselves what has gone wrong, in the operating conditions under which
they went wrong”

A : 180 cinema.
B @ You what?

Recasts are complex discourse structures that have been said to contain
positive evidence (a_model of the correct form), and negative feedback

an environment where the positive evidence is enhanced (because of
juxtaposition). If learners do not selectively attend to and recognize the
negative feedback contained in recasts, then the documented contribution
of recasts to learning might be attributed to the positive evidence
they contain, or to the enhanced salience of the positive evidence.

2. Negative evidence : information that 16 provided to learners concerning

the incorrectness of an utterance

More recently, with an increase in constructivist approaches to the study
of language, we have seen a shift in patterns of research. The shift has not
been so much away from the generative/cognitive side of the continuum,
but perhaps better described as a move even more deeply into the essence
of language. Two emphases have emerged: (a) Researchers began to see
that language was one manifestation of the cognitive and affective ability
to deal with the world, with others, and with the self. (b) Moreover, the
generative rules that were proposed under the nativistic framework were
abstract, formal, explicit, and quite logical, yet they dealt specifically with

The Interaction Hypothesis suggests a number of ways in which interactions
can contribute to language acquisition. More specifically, it suggests that
when interactional modifications lead to comprehensible input via the
decomposition and segmenting of input, acquisition is facilitated; that when
learners receive feedback, acquisition is facilitated; and that when learners
are pushed to reformulated their own utterances, acquisition is promoted.

The above dialogue shows that the interaction has lead to successful

communication, but that it does not contribute to the (1) of the

past tense, a morphological feature. Thus, successful communication
takes place without the learner needing to modify his or her output by
incorporating the past tense marker, showing that not all (2) [~

output is in fact modified.

06. Read the following and answer the question. [ 기 입 형 ]

A

Negotiation strategies

— Clarification requests: any expression that elicits clarification of the
preceding utterance.

— Recasts: any expression that rephrase an utterance by changing one
or more of its sentence components while still referring to its central
meanings.

— Comprehension checks: any expression designed to establish whether
the speaker’s own preceding utterance has been understood by the
addressee.

— Confirmation checks: any expression immediately following the
preceding speaker’s utterance intended to confirm that the utterance
was understood or heard correctly.

Contrastive Analysis 15 an approach to the study of SLA which involves

predicting and explaining learner problems based 00 3 comparison of L1
and L2 to determine similarities and differences. The CAH claimed that the
principal barrier to second language acquisition is the interference of the
first language system with the second language system. The goal of CA is
to isolate what needs to be learned and what does not need to be learned
in a second language learning situation.

differences between L1 and L2. 10 addition, regardless of the features of the
students’ first languages, they appear to go through similar variable stages in
the second language. As a result of the finding that man aspects of learners’
language could not be explained by the CAH, a number of researchers began
to take a different approach to analysing learners’ errors. This approach,
which developed during the 1970s, became known as ‘error analysis’ and
involved detailed description and analysis of the kinds or errors second

language learners make.

igm 0000

Positive Transfer vs Negative Transfer
Transfer means that the transfer of elements acquired in L1 to the target L2.
* Positive(facilitating) Transfer : when the same structure is appropriate in both
languages.
* Negative(interference) Transfer : when the L1 structure is used 10 appropriately
in the L2.

Chapter 03 언어 분 석 및 중 간 언어 77

## Page 78

Chapter

A [읽언혀분삭딪증쵸삐

)
h In the Classroom

Contrastive analysis is based on behaviorism. It considers L1 to be mainly
in interference to the mastery of L2. 10 order to become proficient in L2,
the habits of L1 need to be “broken” before the habits of L2 can become
firmly established. Thus, audiolingualism its most well-known manifestation,
presented us with mim-mem drills and practice with minimal pairs such
as chew and shoe (for Spanish speakers), glass and grass (for Korean
speakers), and dialogues to be memorized 50 that students could avoid
errors 10 016 new language and take on its proper forms.

Students who learn English as a foreign language already have a deep
knowledge of at least one other language, and where L1 and English
come into contact with each other there are often confusions which
provoke errors in a learner’s use of English. This can be at the level of
sounds: Korean, for example, does not have a phonemic distinction
between /r/ 300 /I/ and Korean speakers may well say lice when they
mean rice. It can be at the level of grammar where a student’s first
language has a subtly different system: our students often have trouble
with article usage and the present perfect because Korean doesn’t have
such a form. Thus, in order to solve the problems, the teacher should
provide students with meaningful minimal pairs practice at the level of
sounds and grammar.

Interlanguage means an intermediate system located somewhere between
the learner’s native language and 6 target language, but governed by its
own unique and coherent internalized rule system that rarely becomes totally

congruent with the system of the second language.

@ &8H|(emergent stage)

The second, or emergent, stage of learner language finds the learner
growing in consistency in linguistic production. The learner has begun to
discern a system and to internalize certain rules. In general the learner is
still, at this stage, unable to correct error when they are pointed out by
someone else.

Backsliding
The emergent stage is characterized by some “backsliding” in which the learner
seems to have grasped a rule or principle and then regresses to some previous

stage.
외 국 어 하^자가 목 표 어 의 규 칙 을 습 득 하 는 방 향 으 로 나 가 는 것 이 아 니 라 더 안 정 되 고 용 이 하 다

.는 경 우 에 출 현 한 다 .

@ 체 계 적 CHAl(systematic stage)

A third stage is a truly systematic stage in which the learner is now
able to manifest more consistency in producing the second language.
While those rules that are stored in the learner’s brain are still not all
wellOformed, they are more internally self-consistent and, of course, they

more closely approximate the target language system.

U-shaped learning
U-shaped patterns reflect three stages of linguistic use.

* Stage 1 : A learner produces some linguistic form that conforms to target-like
norms (error-free)

* Stage 2 : A learner appears 10 lose what he or she knew at Stage 1, deviating
from the target language norms.
* Stage 3 . This stage looks just like Stage 1 10 that there is again correct the

target language usage.

88

h In the Classroom

Feedback

@ Explicit Correction : A teacher provides the correct form, he or she
clearly indicates that what the student had said was incorrect, for
example, “Oh, you mean....” “You should say....”

@ Recasts ： A teacher reformulates all or part of 3 student’s utternance,
minus the error. Recasts are generally implicit.

® Clarification requests : A teacher indicate 10 students either that their
utterance has been misunderstood or that the utterance is ill-formed
in some way and that a repetition or a reformulation is required. A
clarification request includes phrases such 35 “Pardon me.”, “What do

you mean by...?” and 80 on.

(@ Metalinguistic feedback contains comments, information, or questions
related 10 the well-formedness of the student’s utterance, without
explicitly providing the correct form.

Ex. Can you find your error?
It’s 3 masculine.

i inine?
Is it femininer (‘(0‘“\(”}\

® Elicitation : Teachers use to directly elicit the correct form from the
student using questions (How do we say X in French?), incomplete
sentences (It’s a....) and asking students to reformulate their utterance.

(® Repetition: A teacher repeat the student’s erroneous utterance. In
most cases, teachers adjust their intonation so as to highlight the

error. € pin-foinkey)

Discourse analysis serves 35 3 device for systematically describing the 11005
of interactions that occur in language classrooms. Discourse analysts give
attention not only to the function of individual utterances but also to how

these utterances combine to form larger discoursal units.

. Halliday’s Seven Language Function

Halliday®] ?7 가 지 언어 기 능 은 언 어 의 기 능 을 중 심 으 로 }는 notional functional
syllabus®| 토 대 가 되 었 으 며 , 언어 교 수 에 있 어 의 사 소 통 적 기 능 을 강 조 하 게 된
계 기 가 되 었 다 .

British discourse analysis was greatly influenced by M.A.K. Halliday’s
functional approach to language whose framework emphasizes the social

functions of language and the thematic and informational structure of speech

and writing.

IRF vs IRE
(1) IRE : 대 부 분 의 교 실 담 화 의 구 조 로 다 음 과 같 다 ,
* The teacher initiates an assertion or asks 3 question.

님 The Student respond_&
* The teacher evaluates, by giving an evaluative statement such as “very

good” or by asking the same or similar question of another student.

REE 학 습 자 들 의 어 휘 력 이 나 문 법 적 능 력 의 내 재 화 정 도 를 알 아 보 기 위 하 여 진 행 되 는 교 실

제 공 한 다 . 그 러 나 이 러 한 IREQ| 담 화 는 교 실 밖 의 의 사 소 통 구 조 를 담 고 있 지 않 으 며 , 또 한 의 미
위 주 의 담 화 형 태 로 발 전 해 나 가 지 않 는 다 .

(2) IRF
학 습 자 의 발 화 에 대 한 평 가 가 아 니 라 , 의 미 위 주 의 담 화 를 확 장 시 켜 나 가 는 것 에 초 점 을 두 고
있 다 . 대 부 분 의 교 실 수 업 의 상호 작 용 은 Initiation — Response — Feedback(RF), 즉 교 사 가
질 문 을 하 고 학 생 들 이 대 답 을 하 고 , 학 생 들 의 대 답에 대 한 피 드 백 의 형 태 를 wct

* The teacher initiates an assertion or asks 3 question.

* The students responds.

* The teacher provides feedback in order to encourage students to think
and 10 perform 31 higher levels (e.g., “Tell me more! Are you saying
that....?)

본 담 화 형 태 에 서 WAHS assisting questions2 학 습 자 가 내 용 과 주 제에 초 점 을 두 어 생 각 하 고

자 신 의 생 각 을 보 다 정 교 하 게 말 할 수 있 도록 유 도 한 다 . 가 령 , 교 사 는 “What 00 you mean by

that?”/ “That's incredible”/ “Could you explain that 3 litle 07067” 이 라 고 ‘assisting question’

등 을 제 시 한 다 . 누 가 말 을 하 고 언 제 해 야 할 것 인 지 를 교 사 에 의 해 통 제 받 는 186 의 담 화

Teacher’s Questions

1. Question types
@ Display question : 3 question which is not a real question but which serves
to elicit language practice.
Ex, 15 this 3 book?
Yes, it’s a book.

Chapter 03 언어 분 석 및 중 간 언어 _ 95

## Page 96

Chapter

96

@ Referential question : 3 question which asks for information which is not
known to the teacher, such as What do you think about animal rights?

In a language classroom, “display” questions were determinant in teacher’s
interaction with learners,and that “referential” questions were more conducive
to the production of lenghtier and more complex responses by learners. It
has been suggested that one way to make classes more communicative is for
teachers to use fewer display questions and more referential questions.

[0 Example |

T ; Last week we were reading “Kee Knock Stan” [title ofa story]. What is “Kee
Knock Stan”, Hyunsoo?

: I cannot understand.

: Yes.

: What do you think the postman at the post office would do?

: I think I would divide it if the letters are to Hong Kong or other places.

: Yes, I think that’s 3 sensible way, right? Good.

영 @ 역역 ㅇ @

Both questions asked by the teacher are “what” questions, but the first one
is 3 “display” question which has only one correct answer, hence “closed”.
The second is a “referential” question with no pre-determined answer, hence
“open”.

. Effective questioning

효 과 적 인 질 문 기 법 은 학 습 자 가 교 사 의 질 문에 대 답할 수 있 도록 유 도 하 는 것 으 로 질 문에 대 한 대 답
을 이 끌 어 내 지 못 할 경 우 교 사 는 즉 각 자 신 의 질 문 을 학 습 자 의 수 준 에 맞 춰 수 정 해 야 할 것 이 다 .
Language teachers in questioning are usually to get their students 10 608386
with the language material actively through speech ;so an effective questioning
technique is one that elicits fairly prompt, motivated, relevant and full responses.
if, on the other hand, their questions result in long silences, or are answered by
only the strongest students, or obviously bore the class, or consistently elicit
only very brief or unsuccessful answers, then there is probably something
wrong.Thus, when teachers fail 10 elicit any response from the learners, they
often need to modify their questions. There are a number of modification

Build-up 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 97

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L‘

devices used by teachers, including syntactic modifications (such as making the
topic salient and decomposing complex structures) and semantic modifications
such as paraphrasing difficult words and disambiguation.

For example, in a language classroom, the teacher read out a sentence
describing a dog. She said “So that’s a very good descriptive sentence.It tells you
exactly what the dog look like. Can you picture the dog?”The teacher realized
that the use of the word “picture” might be a bit beyond the pupils’ability level.
Therefore, she modified the question to “If I were to ask you to draw the dog,
would you be able to draw the dog?”As a result of her lexical modification, the
students immediately responded 10 chorus by saying “yes, yes”.

3. Criteria for effective questioning

@ Clarity : 학 습 자 가 질 문 의 내 용 을 명 확 하 게 인 식 하 고 대 답 을 & 수 있 는 질 문 인 가 ?

@ Learning value : 학 습 자 료 와 관 련 하 여 학 습 자 의 사 고 등 을 자 극 하 여 앞 으 로 의 학 습 에 =8
이 되 는 질 문 인 가 , 또 는 학 습 자 료 와 관 련이 없 거 나 , 도 움 이 되 지 않 고 시 간 만 메 우 는 형 식 의 질
문 인 가 ?

@ Interest : 학 습 자 가 흥 미 있 어 하 고 도전 의 식 을 불 태 우 며 , 답 변 하 고 자 하 는 열 의 를 불 러 일 으
키 는 질 문 인 가 ?

@ Availabilty : 수 업 을 하 고 있 는 모 든 학 생 들 이 대 답 을 할 수 U= 질 문 인 가 또 는 실 력 이 뛰 어 난
학 생 들 (『"6 more 30%30060) 만 대 답 을 할 수 U= 질 문 인 가 ?

® Extension : 학 습 자 들 에 게 보 다 확 장 된 답 변 및 다 양 한 답 변 을 유 도 하 는 질 문 인 가 ?

3. Coherence & Cohesion

(1) Coherence(S&&
웅 집 력 은 텍 스 트 를 구 성 하 는 중 요 한 요 소 중 의 하 나 로 텍 스 트 의 문 장 들 이 서 로 연 관 되 어
논 리 적 일 관 성 을 유 지 하 는 지 의 문 제 이 다 . Cohesive 060106( 결 합 장 치 ) 가 없 어 도 하 나 의
FA(topic) 하 에 문 장 들 이 논 리 적 관 계 를 이 루 고 있 다면 응 집 력 이 있 는 텍 스 트 (0066856
text)7} 될 수 있 다 .

If a text is coherent, it makes sense. The coherent sentences 316 related

to each other, and relate in our background knowledge of the world. The
following text is coherent:
Ex. We want you to be delighted with this facility. If there is a fault with the
toilet please call extn 1071 on the nearest white courtesy phone.
The language and organization of the text are appropriate, especially
when we know that the text was written to be read in an airport restroom.

Moreover, the two sentences of the text have a logical relation.

(2) Cohesion(Z &)
결 함 력 이란 글 의 요 소 들 이 연 관 되 어 있 는 정 도 로 주 로 단 어 의 반 복 , 상 위 단 어 , 인
대 명 사 , 지 시 대 명 사 의 사 용 등에 의 해 결 정 된 다 . 결 합 력 을 응 집 력 을 보 완 하 는 요 소 이 지
결 합 력 이 있 다 고 해 서 반 드 시 응 집 력 있 는 66 파 라 고 할 수 는 없

If a text is cohesive, its elements 316 connected. Cohesion is the use of

]

뚜 에

grammatical and lexical 016315 to achieve connected text, either spoken
or written. Unlike coherence, which different readers or listeners may
experience to varying degrees, cohesion is a stable property of text. And

while cohesion may help make a text coherent, it cannot guarantee it.

09. The following is a conversation extract from a communication-oriented
middle school English classroom. They are discussing the demographic
statistics of Asian countries. Examine the extract and follow the directions
below. [2010 학 년 도 2 차 기 출 문제 변 형 ]

Teacher : What happened to the birth rate in Korea over the last few
years?

Student A : It was | fallen by 2.4%.

Student B : | It was dropped.

Teacher : Okay. Then, what about the birth rate in Singapore?

Student A : The rate was also dropped.

Define each feedback type and give explanation about the feedback.

(1) Definition of the type of feedback and explain the feedback:

04. (1) interlanguage (2) cognitive

08. Student 1 belongs to systematic developmental stage of interlanguage
because she can correct her error, following the teacher’s feedback. (or the uptake
야 Student 1 follows 6 teacher’s feedback.)

09.
(1) The first type of feedback can be given like “Oh, yes, the rate also dropped”.
This type of feedback is “recast” which is defined as an implicit feedback that
reformulates 311 or part of a student’s utterance, minus error.

(2) The second type of feedback which can be used here is “repetition”. Repetition
occurs when the teacher repeats the student’s erroneous utterance in isolation.

Further reading

Flvs FD

A field independent (FI) style enables you to distinguish parts 001
a whole, to concentrate on something (like reading a book in a noisy
train station), to analyze separate variables without the contamination
of neighboring variables. On the other hand, too much FI may result
10 cognitive “tunnel vision”: you 566 only the parts and not their

relationship 10 the whole. “You can’t see the forest for the trees,” 35 the
.sayihg goes. Seen in this light, development of a field dependent (FD)
style has positive effects: you perceive the whole picture, the larger
view, the general configuration of a problem or idea or event. It is clear,
then, that both FI and FD are necessary for most of the cognitive and
effective problems we face.

* like 10 analyze information into its | * like to deal with information structures
component parts and distinguish as wholes

the essential from the inessential * are sociable and work well in groups
* prefer to find solutions 10 problems | « have difficulty discerning a figure
for themselves apart from the field

* are more particularistic and analytic |+ are more global and holistic in pro-
cessing new information

(3) 모 호 함에 대 한 BZ(ambiguity tolerance)

외 국 어 학 습 시 모 국 어 와 다 른 언어 규 칙 이 나 문 화 에 대 해 관 용 적 인 태 도 를 취 해 야

It entails an ability to deal with ambiguous new stimuli without frustration

and without appeals to authority. Successful language necessitates tolerance
of such ambiguities, at least for interim periods or stages, during which time

ambiguous items are given a chance to become resolved.

110 Build-up 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 111

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ F

@
e Further reading

Reflectivity vs Impulsivity ‘,

One important learning style is speed, that is, how slow or fast
someone is. If you prefer doing things more slowly, your style is
reflective. If you like to do things fast, your style is impulsive. Reflective
people usually like to think quite a lot before making a decision. They
need to be certain of the right answer before trying it out. They slowly
move ahead 11 logical steps. They’re usually slower readers, who want to
make sure that they understand what they’re reading before continuing.
Consequently, they usually make fewer comprehension errors in reading.
On the other hand, impulsive learners are faster thinkers. They usually
enjoy making guesses. They are also willing to be wrong sometimes.
They are usually fast readers since they often ignore words or phrases
that they don’t immediately understand and just continue reading. While
they make more errors in reading, they are often good at guessing the
meanings of words and sentences. Both styles have advantages and

disadvantages.

* More accurate speaking * More willing to speak in class

* Faster reading
* Better in timed tests

Advantages | * More accurate reading
* Think carefully before acting

* Wait too long to speak * Less accurate in speaking
Disdvantages | * Read more slowly * Less accurate in reading
* Slower in timed tests * Act without thinking enough

@ AlZt&(visual style) vs ZZt&i(auditory style)
Visual learners tend to prefer reading studying chart, drawings, and other
graphic information, while auditory learners prefer listening to lectures

and audiotapes.

she speaks English, she likes 10 plan what she_ is going to say, 50

Question for clarification

In today’s English class, Minji and her partner were asked to read a newspaper
article and retell the story to each other. Their performance was video-recorded.

Minji wrote the following in her learning log after watching the video.

Minji’s Learning Log

Mistakes and difficulties I had during the task

The newspaper article had a lot of new words that I've never seen
before. / was worried if I could accurately retell the story.

Strategies I used to complete the task

Since I didn’t have time 70 look up words in the dictionary, / had to
guess their meanings based on the context. I thought I understood the
story. When I didn’t have enough words to describe it, I simply used
Korean words.

Overall assessment of my performance on today’s task

I paused a lot without speaking while I was telling the story because
1 didn’t know what to say in English. When I was listening, / didn’t
understand my partner’s story clearly. But I didn’t ask ber to repeat it
because / wasn't sure if it was okay.

Strategies I will practice

In the past, I wrote down new words at least ten times to memorize
them. It didn’t work very well, but I don’t know bhow else I can remember
the words. I will try to read more so I can learn more new words.

© She used metacognitive strategies 10 evaluating her learning process.

Motivation
개 념
동 기 란 특 정 행 동 을 유 발 , 유 지 시 키 는 욕구 및 감 정 을 말 한 다 .
Motivation concerns factors that energize behavior and give it direction.
More specifically, it concerns:
* the choice of a particular action

* the persistence with it

* the effort expended on .

Build-up 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 119

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L‘

(infegratie-)
@ 통 합 적 571 : 목 #( 문 화 에 통 합 , 사 회 적 일 원 이 되 고 자 하 는 욕 구 에 의 한 동 기
Integrative motivation is based on interest in learning L2 because of
a desire to learn about or associate with the people who use it or an

intention to participate or integrate in the L2-using speech community.

(ingTyumentn!) /.

@ 내 적 S7(intrinsic motivation) : 내 적 보 상에 의 한 언 어 학 습
Learners 316 studying because they want to do it or they have 306
their own choice to learn. They don’t need 3 reward from someone else
to do well.
@ 외 적 57 |(extrinsic motivation) : 외 적 보 상 을 기 대 하 며 언 어 학 습 을 하 는 경 우
External motivation 15 when other influences such as teachers or school
requirements, push learners to study L2. In this case, learners often need
to receive rewards such as good grades, high scores, and praise. Without
rewards, you may not be motivated enough to study L2 very hard.

2. Self Confidence (Self—esteem)
자 신과 자 신 의 능 력 에 대 한 자 신 감 은 제 9 언 어 습 득 에 서 중 요 한 요 소 로 방 어 기제
(Inhibition), 위 험 감 수 (018-180908), 1137 (Anxiety) 등과 영 향 을
Self-esteem refers to the degree to which individuals fee confident and
believe themselves to be significant people. Closely related to the notion
of self-esteem is the concept of inhibition and language ego, risk-taking,
anxiety, empathy and so on.

(Gilence ]뻬깨유갯)
Inhibition & Language 690

학 습 자 는 외 국 어 학 습 시 자 신 감 과 자 아 를 보 호 하 기 위 한 e 99 (Inhibition)
이 라 는 방 어 기 제 를 사 용 한 다 . 보 다 효 과 적 인 언 어 습 득 을 위 해 서는 억 압 의 기 제 를

communication gameg 진 행 할 수 있 다 .

The extent to which individuals build defences to protect their egos.
People vary in how adaptive their language egos are, i.e., how able they are
to deal with the identity conflict involved in L2 learning. When people began
to speak their native language, they have learner to understand themselves
in their own language. The close connection between language and ego is
called language ego: the way language helps you to understand and express

yourself.

Learner anxiety 15 016 feeling 이 worry, nervousness, and stress. Anxiety is

@ Facilitative (helpful) Anxiety motivates learners 10 fight the new learning
task, prompting them to make extra efforts to overcome their feelings of
T
anxiety.
@ Debilitative (harmful) Anxiety causes the learners to flee the learning task

in order to avoid the source of anxiety.

(4) Extroversion & Introversion
9|4 (extroversion) 2 만 족 감 이 나 자 긍 심 을 타 인 으 로 부 터 얻 어 내 러 는 성 향 으 로 서
말 하 기 와 쉐《}』 25 5o 납리하며 W& (introversion)> 자 아 로 부 터 이 를 얻 어

Extroversion 18 the extent to which 3 person has 3 deep-seated need to
receive ego enhancement and self-esteem from others. On the other hand,
introversion is the extent to which a person derives a sense of wholeness and
fulfillment apart from a reflection of this self from other people. Extroverts
are sociable, risk-taking, lively, and active; introverts are quiet and prefer

non-sociable activities.

Jin-soo is a high school student learning English in Korea. For him,
studying and learning English does not feel like a burden. He likes to
receive and produce English whenever he can. In addition, he feels that
learning English is important because it will allow him to converse and
be with various international speakers of English.

@

Hye-ri is a university student who is learning English in Korea. She is taking
a course in English literature taught by a Canadian professor. Even though
she sometimes does not understand everything that the professor says in
the lectures, it does not bother her. During her group study meetings with
her classmates, she feels nervous when she speaks in English but tries to
speak whenever she can. She wants to do well in English because it is
important for her to show her ability to her family and friends.

€}

Mi-jin is an office worker and she is presently studying English in
Australia for a period of six months. She is taking an English class
at a language institute there. For Mi-jin learning English is important
because she thinks that it will add to her social status back in Korea. In
relation to classroom activities 31 the institute, she enjoys participating
in small group speaking activities, particularly jigsaw tasks. The high
level of challenge presented by the tasks makes her feel nervous, but
this tension pushes her to do well in them. Concerning the reading
activities, she doesn’t like reading something 10 English because she
feels that she should always look up unfamiliar words in a dictionary.

© a. Jin-soo is both intrinsically and integratively motivated.

b. Hye-ri is extrinsically motivated and engages in risk-taking behavior.

으 Mi-jin is extrinsically motivated and has 3 high willingness to
communicate.

d. Mi-jin experiences facilitative anxiety, and she has 3 low tolerance of
ambiguity when reading.

Teaching Procedure

Step 1 : The teacher asks students where they went last summer and
what they took on their journey. Then she writes down the following
sentences on the board and explains the difference in form and
meaning between the two sentences using the terms “past tense” and
“past participle (pp)”.

I took a light jacket.

I should have taken a warm jacket.

Step 2 : The teacher hands out a story from 3 magazine that includes
the target structure “should+have+pp”. She asks students to read the
story carefully and look for the examples of “should+have+pp” in the
given text. ( 네 . Thotine)

Kate travelled across the Australian desert. She made no
preparations. She didn’t take a map, and she didn’t take
@ cell phone. Soon after she set off, she 807 lost and got
trapped in a flash flood. Later, looking back on it, she
said, “2 should have taken a map. I should have taken a
cell phone...."

Step 3 : The teacher asks students 10 think about their own previous
journey and complete the worksheet below.

Worksheet

I on r?ly I should have
journey. on my journey.

1 took @ light jacket on 1 should have taken a warm
my journey. Jacket on my journey.

e

Step 4 : The teacher asks students 10 write 3 story using the sentences
they have produced in the worksheet above, and to share their writings
with the partners.

> a. Metalinguistic terms are used to help students understand the target

126

structure in Step 1.
b. Students are encouraged to find the target structure in the text in Step 2.
c. Students connect the target structure to their personal experiences in
Step 3.
d. Students are asked to proceduralize the target structure with a focus

on meaning in Step 4.

Acculturation means the process of becoming adapted 10 3 new culture.
According to Acculturation Model, second language learning involves the
acquisition of a second identity and requires learning the culture of that
community and adapting to those values and behavioral patterns. The extent to
which learners acculturate depends on two sets of factors: 500131 distance and
psychological distance, which limit acculturation, and thus inhibit L2 learning.

) 햇/(프힌` distance : The cognitive and affective proximity of two cultures that
( come im; contact within an individual.

@ Psychological distance : The extent to which individual learners are

comfortable with the learning task, therefore, a \pe/rrsg_r)l_a\l rather than a

group dimension.

- Further reading

Sapir-Whorf Hypothesis ... . @ -2 Ahaugint

The Sapir-Whorf hypothesis suggests that both thought and language
are determined by« cult—} For example, culturally determined phenomena
“such 38 the time 아 events of the color or shape of objects might become
especially important for grammar learning in a given language, and, by
extension, 10 thinking as well. Known 35 linguistic_determinism, this
position refers to the idea that people’s thought processes are culturally
determined by the features of the language they speak. However,
research indicates that the strong version 아 the Sapir-Whorf hypothesis,
that language determines though, is unsupported whereas the weak
version has significant implications for intercultural communication. If a

concept exists in one language but cannot be expressed easily in another,

Chapter 04 학 습 자 변인 . 129

## Page 130

Chapter

130

- Further reading

학 습 자 변인

this difference may have an impact on cognitive style 300 the 6356 of
cross-cultural communication involving the concept. For example, it has
been suggested that Chinese or Japanese ESL/EFL learners might avoid
using articles since their languages lack this grammar form. Likewise,
teachers must be aware of learners’culturally determine rhetorical pattten
preferences when teaching academic writing in ESL/EFL classes.

Age & Acquisition

결 정 적 시기 7Hd(The Critical Period Hypothesis)oil 따 르 면 인 간 이 언 어 를 학 습 하 는 데 보 다
용 이 하 게 습 득 할 수 있 는 시 기 는 생 물 학 적 으 로 열 정 퇴 어 있 으 며 , 이 시 기 가 지 나면 언 어 습 득 이
점 점 어 려 워 진 다 . 。

According to the Critical 86000 Hypothesis, animals including humans
are genetically programmed to acquire certain kinds of knowledge and
skill at specific times in life. Beyond those critical periods, it is either
difficult or impossible to acquire those abilities. With regard to language,
the CPH suggests that children who are not given access to language
in infancy and early childhood will never acquire language if these
deprivations to on for too long.

1. 신 경 학 적 인 ZB(behaviorism view) — Hemispheric Lateralization

사 춘 기 이 전 의 어 린 아 이는 뇌 의 유 연 성 으 로 인 해 모 국 어 뿐 아 니 라 제 2 언 어 를 &5
있 다 . 그 러 나 사 춘 기 이 후 의 학 습 자 는 뇌 편 중 화 가 완 료 되 면 서 좌 뇌 위 주 의 학습을 하 게
되 므 로 제 2 언 어 학 습 에 서 원 어 민과 같 은 발 음 을 쉽 게 습 득 하 지 못 하 며 언 어 학 습 을 지 나 치 게
분 석 적 인 과 업 으 로 수 행 한 다 .

The plasticity of the brain prior to puberty enables children 10
acquire not only their first language but also a second language and
that possibly it is the very accomplishment of lateralization that makes

it difficult for people to be able ever again to 23517 304416 fluent
control of a second language, or at least to acquire it with what is
called “authentic” (nativelike) pronunciation.

2. 인 지 적 Z3(cognitive view)

인 간 의 인 지는 생 후 16 년 동 안 급 속 도 로 발 달 하 며 성 인이 된 후 에 는 그 속 도 가 줄 어 든 다 .
As the child matures into adulthood, the left hemisphere (which

controls the analytical and intellectual functions) becomes more

dominant than the right hemisphere (which controls the emotional

functions). It is possible that the dominance of the left hemisphere

contributes to a tendency to overanalyze and to be too intellectually

centered on the task of second language learning.

》 평 형 화 (00 내 109000
인 지는 불 평 형 에 서 평 형 으 로 발 달 하 는 데 형 식 적 조 작 기 에 이르러서떵좇상태룰 이 룬 다 . 이 때
. 임 지 적 불 평 형이 언 어 습 득 의 동 기를 제 공 한 다 .

Conceptual development is 3 process of progressively moving from
states of disequilibrium to equilibrium and that periods of disequilibrium
mark virtually all cognitive development up through age fourteen
or fifteen, when formal operations finally are firmly organized and
equilibrium is reached. It is conceivable that disequilibrium may provide
significant motivation for language acquisition: language interacts with
cognition to achieve equilibrium.

08. Read the following <A> and its teaching implication in <B>. Answer the
question. [ 서 술 형 ]

A

The effect of age on second language acquisition has been extensively
documented, the issue being whether younger learners acquire a second
language more efficiently and effectively than older learners. Research
to date has not conclusively settled the issue of age one way or another,
largely because the issue 15 more complex than it might seem 31 first.
10 order 10 investigate this issue, it is necessary to distinguish between
the effect of age on the route of acquisition, the rate, and ultimate
attainment. Reviews of the available literature concluded that, while age
does not alter the route of acquisition, it does have a marked effect on
the rate and ultimate success.

As shown above, the research argues that the starting age normally
affect how rapidly the learners acquire the language. Also, younger
learners are likely to end up being more proficient than older learners.
Generally, however, the same target language items are acquired in the

same order at which learners are

exposed to the target language for the first time.

성 공 적 인 제 9 언 어 학 습 을 위해 교 사 는 학 습 자 들 에 게 적 절 한 학 습 전 략 을 세 울 수

Much of 06 work of researchers and teachers 00 the application of both
learning and communication strategies to classroom learning has come to
be known generically as strategy-based instruction or as learner strategy
training. Learner strategies are the key to learner autonomy, and that one of
the most important goals of language teaching should be the facilitation of

that autonom&

Core Principles

Training students to use particular learning strategies indeed improves
their language performance. If so, how can teachers make use of learning
strategies? The principle goal as a teacher, is to equip the students with sense
야 what successful language learners do to achieve success and to aid them

developing their own unique, individual pathway to success.

In my English class, I do not want to speak because I cannot say everything
correctly. Actually, in English I do not feel 35 intelligent, friendly, and charming-as-
I feel 11 my Korean language. So, when I speak in English [
1ﬁakir_|g mistakes or losing face. When I make mistake, I get upset and feel that I

have failed. . (. \w\gmﬂ ego 4 ( 906110 anxiety 1)

In my English class I usually like to think quite a lot before

Jisuk

10 class 3 teacher should first identify whether the learning styles (preference) .

Chapter 05 Strategy—based Instructon 143

## Page 144

Chapter

05 Strategy—based Instruction

For example, the three students mentioned above have learning problems
caused by their learning styles or personalities. First, 10 the case of Jiwon, she
shows personal factors which could have negative effects on language success.
For example, Jiwon has 3 very low level of self-confidence about her ability in
English, resulting in high inhibition or the building of defenses around her ego.
Also, she does not take the risk of making mistakes. These traits she has will
make her seldom speak in class unless the teacher calls on her. Even though she
is pushed to speak in English, her pronunciation is not very clear resulting in
miscommunication. Second, the others, Mina and Jisuk have difficulties in terms
of learning styles. In the case of Mina, since she reflects excessively. she thinks
of the right word or grammar pattern and wants to get everything right before
she attempt to speak or write.Thus, she is unwilling to take risks. On the other
hand, Jisuk has a high left-brain preference, which focuses on the small pieces of
English such 35 grammar, vocabulary and pronunciation. Thus, he cannot look at
the whole meaning or intentions of the writer.

To solve these problems the three students have, 3 teacher can plan a learner

training in class which aims to compensate for a certain style or personality-

weakness. For Jiwon, a teacher should sequence techniques from easier to more
weaxness. from eas;i

another, Mina, a teacher should plan small group techniques, role playing and
fluency techniques 80 that she can focus on what she wants to say without
worrying about making mistakes.The last student, Jisuk, can 26 taught to get the
gist of what he is reading through skimming tasks or retelling stories.

2. Good Language Learner Strategies

(1) Strategy 1 : They find 3 learning style that suit them.

Good language learners become aware of the type of L2 learning that suits
them best. Through they conform to the teaching situation to start with, they

soon find ways of adapting or modifying it to suit themselves.

144 Build-up 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 145

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L“

For example, a successful learner who is not a risk-taker and is left-brain
dominant and somewhat intolerant of ambiguity recognizes her dominant
traits and resolves to force herself to take more risks, to balance her
brain, and to adopt a more tolerant attitude toward language she doesn’t

understand.

(2) Strategy 2 : They involve themselves 10 language learning process

They do not passively accept what is presented to them, but go out to
meet it. Thy participate more in the classroom, whether visibly or not. They
take the initiative and devise situations and language learning techniques for

themselves.

(3) Strategy 3 : They develop an awareness of language both 35 system and 35

communication

They are conscious not only that language is a complex system of rules,
but also that it is used for a purpose; they combine grammatical and

pragmanc competence. Whlle many learn lists of vocabulary consciously,

many also seek out opportumtles to take part in conversations in the second

language.

(4) Strategy 4 : They pay constant attention to expanding their language knowl-
edge.

They are not content with their knowledge of a second language, but are
always tying to improve it. They make guess about things they do not know;
they check whether they are right or wrong by comparing their speech with
the new language they hear; they ask native speakers to correct them.

(5) Strategy 5 』 They take into account the demands that L2 learning imposes

They realize that L2 learning can be very demanding. It is painful to expose
themselves in the 12 classroom by making foolish mistakes. They perseveres

in spite of these emotional handicaps.

## Page 146

Chapter

—4 I:EI Strategy—based Instruction

3. How to Teach Strategies in the Classroom

@ Teach strategies through interactive techniques
Many strategies are related to, and actually become, the out ward
manifestation of styles. Here are some suggestions for creating an
atmosphere in the classroom in which students fell comfortable and are

encouraged to develop their own strategies.

ㅡ

. To lower inhibitions: Play guessing games and communication
games, do role-plays and skits, sing songs, have them share their

fears in small groups.

2. To encourage risk-taking: Praise students for making sincere
efforts to try out language, use fluency exercises where errors are

not corrected at that time.

3. 10 build students’ self-confidence: tell students explicitly(verbally
and nonverbally) that you do indeed believe in them.
4. To help them 6 develop intrinsic motivation: remind them

about the rewards for learning English, play down the final
examination in favor of helping students to see rewards for
themselves beyond the final exam.

5. To promote cooperative learning: direct students to share their

knowledge, play down competition among students.

- 6. 10 encourage them to use right-brain processing: use movies
and tapes in class, have them read passages rapidly, do skimming

exercises.

. 7. To promote ambiguity tolerance: encourage students to ask you
and each other, deal with just a few rules at a time.

146 . 844040 영 어 교 육 론

## Page 147

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ k4

8. To help them use their intuition: praise students for good )
guesses, do not always give explanations of errors, correct only .

selected errors.

| 9. To get students to make their mistakes work for them: let |
students catch and correct each other’s errors, do not always give .

them the correct form.

10. 10 get students 60 set their own goals: explicitly encourage or |
direct students to go beyond the classroom goals, have them make .
lists of what they will accomplish.

@ Use compensatory techniques

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L—-

When the appropriate moments occur in the class, teachers need
to seize the opportunity to teach students how to learn. By doing so,
teachers will increase opportunities for strategic investment in their

learning process.

g 0000000000000

Learning strategies are divided into what have come to be known as direct or
cognitive strategies, which learners apply directly to the language itself, and
indirect or metacognitive strategies, in which learners manage or control their
own learning process.
(@ Direct strategies include a number of different ways 이

* remembering more effectively

* using all cognitive processes

* compensating for missing knowledge
@ Indirect strategies include

* organizing and evaluating learning

* managing emotions

* learning with others

4, Different Models of SBI

) As part of a standard communicative methodology, teachers help students
to become aware of their own style preferences and the strategies that
are derived from those styles. Through checklists, tests, and interviews,
teachers can become aware of students’ tendencies and then offer advice
on beneficial in-class and extra-class strategies.

@ Teachers can embed strategy awareness and practice into their pedagogy.
As they utilize such techniques 35 communicative games, rapid reading,
fluency exercises, and error analysis, teachers can help students both

consciously and subconsciously to practice successful strategies.
