@ Certain compensatory techniques are sometimes practiced to help
students overcome certain weaknesses. Therefore teachers need to
provide diagnostic instruments and procedures for determining students’
references, and exercises that help students to overcome certain blocks or
to develop successful strategies they are weak.

@ Teachers can use textbooks that include strategy instruction as part of a

content-centered approach.

K —

Developing Students Seli—-Awareness of Styles

@ Informal self-checklists
One effective way 10 instill student awareness of successful styles is through
an informal self-checklist that students fill out and then discuss.

(@ Formal personality and cognitive style tests
If formal personality or cognitive style tests are available. teachers might try
using them but with caution because these tests are often culturally biased
and have difficult language.

® Readings, lectures, and discussions
Another way of encouraging self-awareness of styles 10 the classroom is for
teachers 10 assign occasional readings, or give mini-lectures or presentations
followed by discussions about successful learning styles.

@ Encouraging “good language learner” behavior
Another form of instilling s\é;lf@s in students is through frequent
impromptu reminders of “rules” for good language learning and encour-
agement of discussion or clarification. Sometimes the little comments from
teachers have the effect of urging students to take charge of their own
destiny by understanding their own styles of learning and capitalizing on
their abilities.

5. Implications

0) 교 사 들 은 학 생 들 뿐 만 아 니 라 자 신 의 680108 스 타 일 과 전 략 을 평 가 해 야 한 다 .

* Teachers could benefit by assessing the learning styles and the strategy
use of their students, because such assessment leads to greater
understanding of styles and strategies.

* Teachers also need to assess their own styles and strategies, 80 that they
will be aware of their preferences and of possible biases.

* Self-report surveys, observations, interviews, learner journals, dialogue

@ 교 사 가 학 생 들 의 학 습 스 타 일 을 잘 알 면 학 생 들 에 게 필 요 한 학 습 전 략 을 제 시 할 수

있 다 .

* The 01016 that teachers know about their students’ style preferences, 116
more effectively they can orient their instruction, as well as the strategy
instruction that can be interwoven into language instruction, matched to
those style preferences.

* Without adequate knowledge about their individual students’ style
preferences, teachers cannot systematically provide the needed
instructional variety.

® 어 떤 한 가 지 교 수 법 이 모 든 학 생 들 에 게 적 합 한 것 은 아 니 라 는 것 을 명 심 해 야 한 다 .

* Styles and strategies help determine 3 particular learner’s ability and
willingness to work within the framework of various instructional
methodologies.

* Instead of choosing 3 specific instructional methodology, teachers would
do better to employ a broad instructional approach. Such an approach
allows for deliberate, creative variety to meet the needs of all students

in the class.

Think Aloud Technique . 뻬쐐몇뻬

To investigate learner strategies, teachers can let learners think aloud 35
thinking processes or strategies they are making used of. For example, while
writing a composition, a student may record his or her thoughts into a tape
recorder during the planning, drafting, and revision of the composition. Later,
the recording may be used to determine the planning or revision processes
used by the student.

1. Strategy Type 1: Metacognitive — Plan your time

Level : Intermediate or above

Time : 50 minutes

Aim : For students to consider ways in which they can learn
English outside the classroom

Preparation : Make photocopies of the task sheet for your class.

Teaching Procedure

| Step 1 : Arouse student interest in the planning task.

Step 2 : Set up the initial pair work and give the students five to ten
minutes to discuss, add to, or modify the list of suggestions. . .

Step 3 : When the 101031 discussion is over, you should facilitate
the setting up of groups. Allow the groups a maximum of |
twenty minutes to complete the planning task. ㅣ

Step 4 : Chair the report-back session 10 which each group |
presents its suggestions. Make OHTs or posters available !
to help the groups present their ideas.

， Task sheet

! Here is 3 list of techniques that people use to help hem learn
。 English outside the classroom:

* memorizing 3 list of words

* reading 3 grammar book

* doing grammar exercises

* reading 3 book or magazine 10 English

* recopying things from their class notebook

* preparing the next unit of the coursebook

Work with a partner and add any others of your own. Tell each
other which ones in the list you find helpful, if any, then tell the
class about the new ones you have added.

Arrange yourselves in groups and take a time period from this list:
* thirty minutes per day for six days a week

* one hour per day for five days 3 week

* two hours per day for four day a week

In your group, plan a program to show how you could make use
of the time to do extra work on your English. Use the ideas from
the earlier list, as well as any others you can think of. Choose one
person to present your plan to the rest of the class.

Teaching Procedure

Step 1 : Learning journals & Interview

Prior 10 the lesson the teacher has been reading the students'
learning journals, where the students regularly write about what
and how they are learning. The teacher has also been interviewing .
the students. .

Step 2 : Decision of the strategy of advance organization

The teacher decides to have students work on the strategy of

advance organization. He thinks that they need to improve their

reading by preview and to skim to get the gist of a_reading .

passage; Learning this strategy will improve their comprehension
“and the speed at which they read.

Step 3 : The teacher models the 456 of the strategy using a_think-
aloud demonstration.

He begins the class with a presentation. He tells students that
they are going to work on a learning strategy called advance
organization. He begins by modelling. He distributed a reading
passage.

‘What I do first is read the title. I try to figure out what the
passage is about. I look at the subheadings and pictures, too, if

there 316 any. I ask myself what I know about the topic and what .
questions I have. Next, I read the first paragraph. I don't read very

word, however. I let my eyes skim it very quickly-just picking out |
what I think 316 the main ideas. I especially look at the content or |

meaning-bearing words-usually the nouns and verbs'. The teacher '
calls out the words that he considers key 10 the first paragraph. 'From |
。 doing these things, I know that this passage 15 about wild horses. |
! I do not know very much about the topic, but from skimming the |
| first paragraph, 1 have gotten the impression that the passage is |
‘ about the challenges of catching and taming wild horses’. |

| Step 4 : The students practice the new learning strategy.

¢ Td like you to practice just this much now. I am going to hand

' Good. Now remember, before you turn the paper over, you are

야 whales being put on the endangered list. “That’s 3 good guess’

Step 5 : The students evaluate their own success in learning the
strategy. They modify the strategy to meet their own

learning needs.

Next the students evaluate how they have done. Some feel
distressed because they still feel that they need to understand very
word. However, others are feeling better because they realize that
their reading assignments need not take as long as they have been
taking. Some students discuss their implementation of the strategy
and how they modified it. All of the students feel that they need a

lot more practice with this new strategy.

(1) Value

학 생 들 은 그 들 이 가 치 있 다 고 여 기 는 과 제에 보 다 동 기 부 여 를 받 는 다 . 따 라 서 교 사 는

학 생 들 의 내 재 적 인 동 기를 유 발 할 수 있 는 과 제 를 제 공 해 야 한 다 .

Students 316 more motivated on tasks that they value. Students may value

insically interesting, because they find it

a task because they find it int
applicable to their lives or both. Teachers try many ways to gain students’
intrinsic interest 10 academic tasks, including using authentic literature,
choosing activities that might be fun for students, and focusing on content

that has cultural relevance.

(2) Expectancy
학 생 들 은 과 업 에 서 성 공 할 수 있 다 는 기 대 감 을 가질 경 우 동 기 부 여 를 더 받 게 된 다 .
그 러 므 로 학 생 들 이 성 공 을 경 험 할 수 있 는 기 회 를 제 공 해 야 한 다 .

Over years of exposure to learning experiences, students develop overall
expectations for success or failure in school. Thus students often have an
overall feeling of being a “good student” or “bad student”. Most high school
students can identify areas that they think they are “good at” and “bad at”.
Each day in a classroom can reinforce a student’s expectations influence
each future encounter in that field. Thus it is critical to ensure that students
experiences successes in the classroom. It is important to have tasks that are
appropriately challenging-not so difficult that students cannot experiences to
appropriate i‘fﬁ‘f_ééj can help students experience success on challenging

amningful tasks and can eventually lead to higher expectations of

learning process.

(3) Self—Efficacy

Chapter 05 Strategy—based Instruction . 159

## Page 160

Chapter

_4 | : E I Strategy—based Instruction

cessfully

Self-efficacy is the specific confidence that one is capable of
completing a_particular task. Self-efficacy is at the core of motivation, self-
é;e—e'rr_l, and self-regulation. The development of self-efficacy is tied closely
with effective use of learning strategies. Students with high self-efficacy have
confidence that they can solve a problem because they have developed
strategies for the task that have worked for them 10 the past. They believe
that the more they learn and practice, the more their capabilities will
improve, and they understand that mistakes are just a part of learning. In
contrast, students with low self-efficacy think that they have inherent low
ability. They believe that effort will reveal their own inability, and they
choose easy tasks to try to avoid making mistakes. Building students’ strategic
awareness can help them see that they are capable of solving problems when
they use the correct tools. In second language research, correlation have
been found between students’ reported learning strategies use and their self-
efficacy, in particular their beliefs about their own effectiveness as language

learners.

(4) Attribution
전 략 기반 수 업 은 학 생 들 에 게 그 들 이 적 절 한 전 략 을 사 용 할 경 우 성 공 할 수 있 다 는 것 을
보 여 줌 으 로 써 학 생 들 의 성 공 혹 은 실 패 에 대 한 관 여 도 (960164000) 를 바 꿀 수 있 다 .

Students’ approach to school tasks can depend on the factors to which they
attribute their success or failure. Less often, students may attribute school
success to effort or, even more appropriately, to effective strategies. Strategies
instruction helps to alter students’ attributions by showing them that they can
succeed if they use appropriate strategies and, conversely, that their failure
can be attributed to the lack of effective strategies use rather than the lack of
ability.

Teachers can identify the strategies that best meet their students’ immediate
needs, they can explore students’ reactions to their instructional approaches,
and they can use classroom research as 3 tool for showing students that

strategies really work.

&

1. Cognitive—Classifying

In the Classroom

Tasks such as the following that require learners to put vocabulary
items into their semantic groups are classification tasks. Classifying helps
learners because it is easier to memorize items that are grouped together
in meaningful ways than trying to remember isolated items.

2. Cognitive—Predicting
Predicting, or looking ahead, helps learners to anticipate what is to
come. This results in more effective learning, because the learners are

adequatelyprepared for the new material.

3. Cognitive—Inductive Reasoning

In an inductive approach to learning, students are given access to
data, and are provided with structured opportunities to work out rules,
principles, and so on for themselves. The idea here is that information
will be more deeply processed and stored if learners are given an
opportunity to work things out for themselves, rather than simply being
told.

3 pairwork Draw a line 10 show where each adverb should go 10 each

sentence.

의 Does it snow in New York? often
d. It is wet in London. always
e. It snows in Sydney. never

Chapter 05 Strategy-based Instruction 163

## Page 164

Chapter

A I:EI Strategy—based Instruction

4, Cognitive—Inferencing

Inferencing involves using what you know to learn something new.
Because learning is basically making links between what is new and
what is already known, inferencing is an extremely important strategy.

— T — T — T < ㅅ ,

Class Work

Writers often suggest what they mean rather than stating it directly.
Readers must then make inferences, or draw logical conclusions, based
on the available information. What inferences can you make about
the slave traders 300 the Ibo people based on the information in the
sentences below?

a. While the slave traders were 10 Africa, they went by the Ibo tribe,
and they found eighteen grown people. They fooled them. They told

5. Interpersonal-Cooperating

When we cooperate, we share ideas and learn with other students. This
principle exploits the old saying that “two heads are better than one.”It is
particularly effective 10 language learning, because students are required
to communicate with each other in order to cooperate.

_—

With a group of three students, consult the information from the tables
above, and choose a city that you would like to visit. Describe to your
class your choice of destination and the reasons your group chose .
Next, as a group, write a letter to its tourist board (addresses below), or
visit a local travel agency to get more information. When you receive
the information, read it, and create a poster advertising your destination.
Include information from the tables in this chapter, as well as photos or

information included in the promotional literature you received in the mail.

6. Linguistic—Practicing
An essential strategy for developing skills is practicing. Practicing means
doing controlled exercises to improve knowledge and skills.

W —_—

The Sound of It :
Understanding Intonation in Negative Questions

People often use statement word order to ask a negative question if they

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L—

7. Linguistic-Listening

A key strategy for learners is listening for key information without trying
to understand every word. This strategy is essential if learners are to cope
effectively in genuine communicative situations outside the classroom. It
is important for learners to realize that native speakers use this strategy
quite naturally when communicating with one another, that it is, in fact,
impossible as well as unnecessary to process every single word in most
listening situations.

—_— e

Listen for Numbers

Read these questions about the story asking How far an How long.
Then listen to the story again and answer the questions.

1. How long are they going to be on vacation?

2. How far is it from Ohio to the Shenandoah National Park?

3. How long are they going to stay at the park?

4. How far is it from the park to Wilmington?

—

5. How long are they going to stay in North Carolina?

6. How far is it from Wilmington to Orando?

(4) Lesson procedure

@ Procedure 1 (Target task and Pedagogical task)

Goal : giving personal information in a job interview

* listening to extracts 아 authentic job interviews giving a handout
* drilling in the use 아 frequency adverbs whole class-listening
comprehension

* exercising in comprehension 아 Wh-questions with do- | pair work

insertion
* analysing the grammar and discourse 아 the interviews ! group work-discussion
* modeling an interview : teacher and student presentation for the task
* role-playing a simulated interview : students 10 pairs pair work-role-play

@ Procedure 2 (3-step)

@ Pre—task : The teacher explores the @c with the class and may
highlight useful words and phrases, helping students to understand the
task instructions. The students may hear a recording of other people

Lask doing the same task.
7”7? ® Task cycle : The students perform the task 10 pairs or small groups while
the teacher monitors from a distance. The students plan how they will

tell the rest of the class what they did and how it went and they then
report on the task either orally or in writing, and compare notes on
what has happened.

© Language focus : The students examine and discuss specific features of
any listening or reading text which they have looked at for the task and
the teacher may conduct some form of practice of specific language

features which the task has provoked.

182

- o B

2011 학 년 도 기 출 \l

Fighting Jet Lag
Pre-task

* The teacher shows visual materials related to jet lag and elicits
experiences from students.

Task Cycle 1

* The teacher asks students to brainstorm, in pairs, ways to overcome
jet lag.

* Pairs select three items from what they have brainstormed.

* Pairs rehearse how to explain their choices and then present their list
to the class with justifications.

Task Cycle 2

* The teacher distributes copies 아 3 magazine article about overcoming jet
lag and has students write down the three ways mentioned in the text.
* Pairs compare their list to that of the article.

Lesson Procedure of PPP model

Integrating grammar using a PPP model (Intermediate level)

Step 1

The teacher uses a generative situation to provide a ¢ontext for the
targeted language. In this case she introduces two characters, Tom and
Viv, and tells the class that when he was young, Tom’s parents were
very strict. Viv, on the other hand, had very easygoing parents. For
example, she says, Tom had 10 help with the housework. But Viv didn’t
have 10 help with the housework. Tom’s parents made him take piano
lesson. He didn’t want to, but they made him. Viv, on 6 other hand,
wanted to have a motorbike. Her parents let her have one... Then, the
teacher asks the class to report back what they can remember her saying
about Tom and Viv. She elicits on to the board the following sentences,

shaping them into their correct form:

D TBLO Teochet does mo obtempd 4o contol 16 100908
@ The 96066 s Judged on  whedher 0+ /ot Jeopness commmmnl Code  Suct erstully,
@ At some  sloges, leovmas o 9000 ol focus 매 lanquage .
T el 100005 1 shaje 000 clnfy wher they word o <o,
© Tocvs on @ Comel oller e on meantsg .,
Chapter 06 2+ 185

## Page 186

Chapter

Step 2

. He had to help with the housework.

. His parents made him take piano lessons.

. He wasn’t allowed 10 stay out late.

. Viv didn’t have 10 help with the housework.
. Her parents let her have a motorbike.

으 내 6 02 N 0

. She was allowed 10 stay out late.

The teacher then elicits paraphrases of each of the six sentences and

writes them on the board. For example:

13. His parents made him help with the housework.
2a. He had to take piano lessons.
3a. They didn’t let him stay out late.

The teacher erases the first set of sentences and asks students, in pairs,
if they can reconstruct them from memory. Students write the sentences
and these are read out and checked in open class.

Step 3

The teacher sets a group-work task. The object is to find out who, in
their groups, went to the strictest, and who to the most easygoing,
school. Students prepare questions to ask each other using the target
language forms and finally report back to the teacher. A general class

discussion follows, as to the merits or not of a strict education.

The lesson is plotted along PPP lines, with Step 1 being the presentation,
Step 2 the (controlled) practice and Step 3 the freer production.

The presentation is a @ one, arguably more Qéa{f}ixﬁg_@j and
piemorable than 3 straight explanation. The practice stages involve the

186 Buildup 영 어 교 육 론 1

## Page 187

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ F

students first of 311 transforming and then réconstructing the model

sentences (Step 2). This is done as a written exercise, allowing students

reflection ;ime/as well as the chance to work collaboratively. It is
probably at the stage when the students are reading out their sentences
that the teacher would start to fine-tune for accurate pronunciation.
The fluency task (Step 3) is still relatively controlled, but by having the
students ask and answer a lot of questions using the target language
there is built-in repetition. Moreover, there is a @m

to the task, ensuring a focus on meaning as much as on form.
U . 5 |

Lesson Procedure of TBI

Integrating grammar using a task-based instruction (Intermediate level)

Step1: @é-task

The teacher introduces the theme by telling a short anecdote about her
school days, which demonstrates, for example, the relaxed approach to
the dress-code operating in her school. She uses this story to check the

meaning of easygoing and its opposite, strict.

5660 2 : 났촉좋】( cycle

She suggest that many people react against a strict upbringing by adopting
very easygoing attitudes as parents, and vice versa. Since there is some
argument about this, she suggests that the class conduct a@ in which
they canvass each other 10 see if there 15 any correlation between previous (meonrgta

outhentic
experience and present attitudes. She organizes the class into pairs 0 (use)

prepare questions, which they write down. Then, the teacher organizes the
pairs of students into groups of four, and asks them to try out their questions (Zptesaction )
on each other, and to make a mental note of the answers. She monitors the
interactions, noting down examples of student productions that could be

improved, but she doesn’t correct them at this point.

Chapter 06 248 . 187

## Page 188

Chapter

188

Step 3 : {?ast-task

@ The teacher asks the class 10 listen 10 3 recording of some fluent

includes various examples of the language of coercion. The teacher

asks some general gist questions about the conversation — for
example, which of the speakers had a strict upbringing, which had an
easygoing one? She then hands out a transcript of the recording, and
replays the tape while they read.

@ Students then study the transcript with a view 10 finding language that
might be useful in the survey task, particularly language related to the
notions of being strict and easygoing. They list these in two columns:
adjectives and verbs. Students work in pairs on this task, and then the

teacher elicits ideas on to the board. For example:

Adjectives Verbs

tolerant I was allowed
he made me
I won't let them

She then asks the class to complete the blank spaces after each verb, and

to make@gneralizatio@_.about the grammar 아 the verbs. She also elicits

the question forms 아 the verb structures: were you allowed to...? etc.

Step 4

The students then return to their survey task — but are first given a
chance to redraft and refine their questions in pairs. They are then paired
off with different students than the ones they were talking to earlier (in
Step 2). The teacher then asks students, working in their original pairs,
to prepare 3 report on their findings, with 3 view to answering the
que;&z)_n: How does upbringing affect ; attitudes? Individual students are

asked to present their report to the class. A general discussion ensues.

Build-up 영 어 교 육 론 1

## Page 189

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L?

The lesson is a task-based one because, rather than being plotted
around a pre-selected item of grammar, the purpose of the lesson is to
achieve a task outcome: in this case, deciding how upbringing affects
attitudes. While this may seem contrived — just as contrived, in fact, as

pre-selecting a grammar item — it could be argued that the task focus
encourages learners to take more Vg;%‘egvtri?eV(ris}g::g\__y_v“ighmt_l]me_i_( language.
They needn’t restrict themselves 10 the teacher’s grammar agenda (as in
Sample lesson 1): theoretically, they could choose any language from
the sample text (Step 3). Finally, and most importantly, a task invests the
《 ㅡ Wor 120019 - velpled, puthertc, 091
lesson with an intrinsic interest, , apart from a concern only for language.
The language is simply a means, ‘not an end 10 itself (as 16 80 often the

case in PPP-type lessons).

It should be clear that this task-based lesson shares many of the
ingredients of the PPP lesson, but that the order is radically different: the

major difference being that the \productBn stage is brought to the front
야 the lesson (Step 2) after an initial introduction to the theme (Step 1).
The lesson starts 10 the deep end, as it were. The production stage 3065
35 3 @ run, where learners attempt to put into words the meanings
they wish to express. The problems they have doing this should motivate
them to look for solutions 10 the sample text (Step 3). That is, they have
an incentive to use the text as a resource, and may be better primed for

(intngic)
noticing features of the text than /f they had just read it for the sake of

reading it. The teacher’s role is 10 guide students (Step 3-@) to notice
features that she herself has diagnos'evd as being misused or underused
in the trial run. Students are then ready, theoretically, to re-attempt the task
(Step 4). As a final push towards accuracy, the report stage (Step 4), in
which the students ‘go public’, imposes an element of foyrrrvleili‘t?‘ that forces

attention on to form.

Immersion model 15 designed to teach both content and language
simultaneously, based on the premise that people learn new languages in

the course of learning other things. The features are as follows:

@ It uses authentic_materials or the same materials used by native

speakers to learn the same content rather than materials specifically
designed for language learners.
® The content instruction is tailored to the @ of the language learners.
© The teachers have knowledge both about content and target language.

In sheltered-language instruction, both native speakers and non-native
speakers of a particular language follow a regular academic curriculum.
For classes with non-native 806360 however, “sheltered” instruction is

geared to students’ developmg second language proﬁc1ency Sheltered-
language instructors suppo:rt their students through the use of particular
instructional techniques and materials. It offers the significant advantage
that second language students do not have to postpone their academic

study until their language control reaches 3 high level.

10 the adjunct model, students enroll 10 3 regular academic course. Then,

students process the language in order to understand the academic

content presented by the subject teacher. The language teacher also helps
students to complete academic tasks.

(& Competency-based Instruction ： D\M(om@ o | (achiciement of cpecific 90015

Competency-based instruction, an effective form 아 content-based instruction

Competency: Returning an item to the store

Learners will be able to

— Explain reasons for returning an item.
— Demonstrate understanding of return policies: a refund, an exchange,
or store credit.

Sample activity
Half of the class is assigned the role of store clear. Each clerk is given store
policies.

— Must have a receipt for a refund.
— Purchase made less than 30 days ago.
— Must have packaging for a refund.

Each of 06 other students 10 class 15 given an item to return; some are given
a receipt, some have item in bos; some have item with no packaging.

Role-play

Students return items to the appropriate store.
Redistribute items and assign new clerk.

Follow-up

Did you get a refund, a new item, or a store credit? Why? Did you get

what you wanted?
What were the store policies and how did they affect you?

0 수 업 모 형

The teacher greets the students and explains that they are about to begin a
new and exciting experience in language learning. She says confidently, “You
won’t need to try to learn. It will just come naturally.”(3 32l 수 업 에 대 한
직 접 적 인 암 시 ) First, you will all get to pick new names-English ones. “It
will 66 fun”, she says. Besides, she tells them they will need new identities
(one they can play with) to go along with this new experience. She shows
the class a poster with different English names printed in color. There are
men’s names 10 one column and women’s names 10 another. She tells them
that they are each to choose a name. She pronounces each name and has the
students repeat the pronunciation. Next, she tells them that during the course
they will create an imaginary biography about the life of their new identity.
But for now, she says, they should just choose a profession to go with the
new name. Using pantomime to help the students understand, the teacher
acts 041 varjous occupations, such 35 pilot, singer, carpenter, and artist. The
students choose what they want to be.

(3) 시 사 점
The teacher should recognize that learners bring certain psychologlcal
barriers with them to the learning situation. She should attempt to
desuggest these. Assuming a new identity enhances students’ feeling of
mnd allows them to be more open. They feel less inhibited since

their performance is really that 아 a different person.

(3) 수 업 모 형

The teacher walks to the front of 06 room, takes 041 metal pointer and
points to a chart hanging above the blackboard. The blocks are arranged
in rows. Each block is a different color. This is a sound-color chart. Each
rectangle represents one English sound. There is a white horizontal line
approximately halfway down 6 chart separating the upper rectangles,
which represent vowel sounds, 601 those below the line, which
represent consonant sounds. Without saying anything, the teacher points
in succession to each of five blocks. Again, no one says anything. The
third time, the teacher does the pointing, he says /a/ as he touches the
first block. The teacher continues and taps the four other blocks of color
with the pointer. As he does this, several students say /e/, /i/, /o/, /u/.

수 업 모 형 _ .

It is the first class of the year and the teacher introduces the 064100 she
will use to study English, “You will not speak at first. Rather, you will just
hsten to me and do as I do. I will give you a command to do something
10 Enghsh and you w1ll do the actions along with me.”

In English the teacher says, “Stand up.” As she says it, she stands up
and she signals for the four volunteers to rise with her. They all stand
up. “Sit down,” she says and they all sit. Then, the teacher issues a new
command, “Turn around”. The students follow the teacher’s example.

As the last step of the lesson, the teacher writes the new commands
on the blackboard. Each time she writes a command, she acts it out. The
students copy the sentences from the blackboard into the notebooks.

7

Audio—Motor Unit

Audio-Motor Unit= 명 령 에 문 맥 적 인 부 분 을 더 함 으 로 써 TPRe| 명 령 과 동 작 들 이 유 의 미 한 연 결 이

이 루 어 지 지 않 는 다 는 단 점 을 보 완 했 다 。 < 640#91001200' commndy)
- - Cupperer TPR's diovibacks )
. Features 롯〈 s

* It includes a particular sequence of commands, all centering on a single topic.
Ex. Topic :Set the table
“go to the cupboard, open the cupboard door, find the largest bowl,
take it out, set it on the table”
* The teacher demonstrates the appropriate responses to the commands,
using whatever realia are available to make the actions comprehensible.
* The students are invited to comply with the commands.

* It can include cultural learning 10 the lessons. Various qstoms)involving
eating, preparing food, telephone conversations, and introductions are
taught through a series of commands given 10 the context of real or pretend
situations. T 2

2. Benefits

* The vocabulary, structures, and syntax of the language used in their lessons
were reinforced by exposure to the audio-motor strategy.

* Students became strongly interested 10 the lessons through 6 physical
acting out of cultural aspects.

* The lessons, even though designed for the development of listening skills,
had 3 real impact on oral production. The teachers noticed increased
spontaneity and better pronunciation, although they admitted the latter was
difficult to verify.

* The nonnative teachers of the various languages felt that they improved
their own skills with the languages they were teaching.

Two middle school English teachers instruct their classes.

Teacher A : “Today I'll going to show you how 02 set a table. Before I
do, I'll pass out spoons, forks, plates, and knives... Now watch what I
do and follow along. I'll putting 3 plate on the table... Now put a plate
on the table. On the table. OK?... Good job! Now I'm putting the fork
on the left side of the plate. [Teacher continues with other utensils.] Well
done! Now, what I'd like you to do is practice setting a table in pairs.
One partner tells the other what to 00 and that partner follows the

commands...”

Teacher B : “Everybody, today we have a mystery to solve. I have
. . ,/_\ . . " s

six picture ¢lues. You will each be given just one picture. Then find 3
partner and eexchange mformauon in your own words. After that, find
another partner and do the same until you have sufficient clues or the
alloted time runs out. After gathering information, you will form groups
of four and come up with a solution to the mystery. There are many
p0551ble solutions. You'll have 15 minutes. Finally, one ‘member from

your 'group will report your group’s solution back to the class.”

@ 수 업 단 계
@ Comprehension (Pre-production)
이 단 계 에 서 학 생 들 은 HE7](silent period)S 가 지며 교 사 로 부 터 이해 가 능 한
언 어 입 력 을 받 아 들 인 다 ,

* During this first stage, the students go through a silent period. They
receive comprehensible input usually from the teachers. Often the
total physical response is used.

* Simple responses to the comprehensible input 37 66 made by
gesturing, nodding, using the L1, answering “yes,” or “no,” giving
names of people or objects as answers to questions.

* A lot of visuals, explanations, and repetitions are used.

* The teacher’s speech is 3 little slower than usual. The intonation
is reasonably normal except that key words receive a bit of extra
emphasis.

* Students 316 not called upon to respond individually. Instead,
questions are directed to the whole group, and one or several can
respond.

. Kégr'ierins are written on the board, perhaps on the second or third
time the students are exposed to them.

® Early speech production
두 번 째 단 계 에 서 학 생 들 은 점 점 발 화 를 시 작 하 면 서 자 신 들 의 중 간 언 어 를
발 달 시 키 고 정 교 화 한 다 . (1)
* The second stage generally begins with 31 extension of many

activities used in the comprehension stage such as the sentence-

completion response, in which a personalized question is asked and

the answer is provided except for one word.

* Students gradually answer “Mike has dress” instead of just “Mike” to
teacher’s question “Who has on a blue dress?”

* The speech at first will contain many errors which should 66 dealt
with only 1nd1rect1y, for example, “Yes, Mike has on the dress
instead 아 “No you should say, Mike has on the dress.”

(© Speech emergence
학 생 들 의 발 화 에 이 미 변 화 가 시 작 되 었 기 때 문에 두 번 째 단 계 와 세 번 째 단 계 를
나 누 는 것 이 다소 모 호 하 지만 이 단 계 에 서는 학 생 들 의 오 류 가 점 점 줄 어 들며
점 점 어 렵 고 도 전 적 인 과 제 를 통 해 서 발 화 를 계 속 확장해 나 간 다 .

* The utterances become longer and more complex. Many errors are still
made but, if enough comprehensible input has been internalized, they
should gradually decrease as the students move toward full production.

* A large number of activities can 66 used that are somewhat more
demanding and challenging but still within reach cognitively: music,
poetry, role-playing, drama, affective activities, and problem-solving.

* Many of the activities can be extended to provide additional opportunities
for development. For example, instead of simply answering questions
about an application formy/the students can now fill one out; instead 아
just following directions, they can begin to write their own sets of simple

directions/to see if others can follow them.

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L‘

Lesson Theme: Body parts and ailments
Student level: beginner level

Teaching Procedure
Step 1: Teacher displays visual of a person with body part labeled.
She begins by pointing to words and saying body parts (students

repeat words only if comfortable with language).

Step 2: Teacher removes labels, distributes them, and has each learner

affix label to correct body part.

Step 3: Teacher removes cards again. Students in class have a set of yes/
no cards (yes one color, no anther color). Teacher points to and says a body
part, sometimes correctly and sometimes incorrectly. Learners hold up yes/
10 card according to whether the teacher said the right or wrong word.

Lesson Theme: Washing A Car
Task type: problem solving
Student level: intermediate level

Teaching Procedure
Step 1: The teacher will guide the students in developing the vocabulary
necessary to talk about the activity. -rag, water, park, bucket, sponge,

driveway

Step 2: Then together the class and instructor create utterances to describe .
the sequence of events to complete the activity. The class might say:

— First I look for a bucket and a sponge or some rags.

ㅡ Then / park the car in the driveway.

— / use the hose 70 wash the car first with water only.

| Step 3: After sequence 15 constructed, the discussion will broaden 10

| include questions 31 discussion concerning the specific activity 10

Q : What are the goals of teachers who use this method?

A : Teachers hope 10 accelerate the process by which students learn 10
use a foreign language for everyday communication. In order to do
this, more of the students’ mental powers must be tapped. This is
accomplished by removing the psychological barriers learners bring
with them to the learning situation.

: How 316 the feelings 아 the students dealt with?

> O

: A great deal of attention is given to studenrs’fee@n;s in this method.
One of the fundamental principles of the method is that if students
are relaxed and confident, they will not need to try hard to learn
‘the language. It will just come naturally and easily. For an example,
students choose target language names on the assumption that a
new identity makes students feel more secure and thus more open
to learning.

Authentic Materials

1. Definition

Authentic materialst 목 표 문 화 권 의 화 자 에 의 해 생 성 된 담 화 및 글 을 의 미 하 는
것 으 로 , 현 재 진 행 되 는 외 국 어 교 실 수 업 에 서 의 학 습 자 료 로 선 택 해 야 할 것 인 지에
대 한 찬 반 이 엇 갈 리 고 있 다 . 따 라 서 교 사 는 학 습 자 료 로 authentic 1856181 을 선 택 할

을 명 확 하 게 파 악 하 고 학 습 자 의 순 준 을 고 려 하 여 , 어 떻 게 authentic

material& 교 실 수 업 에 서 제 시 해 야 할 것 인 지 를 생 각 해 야 한 다 .

There 316 number of definitions related to authentic materials; @ an
authentic text is a stretch of oral language, produced by a real speaker
or writer for a real audience and designed to convey a real message of
some sort @ authentic texts (either written or spoken) are those which
are designed for native speakers; they are text designed not for language
students, but for the speakers of 116 language in question, 30 @ 3 rule of

thumb for authentic specially produced for propose of language teaching.

SITUATION 2: CHOOSING A DAY TOUR

Work in pairs or groups of three.

You are staying in Eastbourne and want to go somewhere for the day.
Look through the following Tour Guide and try to find somewhere to go.
One person starts: ‘What do you fancy doing today?’

Hampton Court
Palace

This magnificent Palace on the banks of the
River Thames was built by Wolsey and pre-
sented 10 King Henry VIl in 1626. At the rear,
large additions were made by Wren for King
William 111, including the attractive Fountain
Court. Of particular interest are also the Great
Kitchen, the Great Vine and the Wine Cellars,
the Maze and the Flower Gardens.

Canterbury

This ancient city whose history can be traced
to Roman and Medieval times is dominated by
the Cathedral, the building of which was started
in the 12th century and continued until the early
15th century. Thomas & Becket was murdered
in the Cathedral in 1170 and from that date until
King Henry ViIl destroyed the Martyr's Shrine
it was a place of pilgrimage from all over
Europe. The Norman Crypt is the largest in the
world.

Depart 1000 Return 2000 Fare £2:25

Depart 0930 Return 1730 Fare £1-65

(1) Interest

Authentic materials 316 taken from real life and are going to be introduced
in the artificiality of 6 classroom. 50 3 teacher has to ensure that the topic
of the authentic materials chosen addresses the needs and interest of the

specific group of students being taught.

(2) Purpose 이 language learning < 64610 Lophe frodemnc Largwoge prficiency )
자 료 를 선 택 하 는 데 있 어 서 학 생 들 이 영 어 ㅎ 이 고 려 되 어 야 한 다 .
The 8031 야 language learning for that particular group of learners should

value of the materials /é a very important consideration. The teacher has to
consider if the information included 10 the materials chosen are of ( lue to

the students.

(3) Cultural appropriateness ( Sovio—curturs) 나다 — leamer variable )
모 국 어 의 문 화 와 상 이 한 문 화 적 배 경 을 지 닌 자 료 를 선 택 할 경 우 , 학 생 들 에 게 그 배 경 을

(4) Language level ( it " Kioshen')
진 정 성 있 는 자 료 는 학 생 들 의 언 어 적 수 준 을 고 려 해 서 선 택 되 어 야 한 다 . 단 언 어 적

While choosing the materials the most important factors is the level of
the language used in the materials. These materials are taken from real life
and not manipulated for the specific purpose of teaching 3 language in a
classroom. The input has to be comprehensible to the students. At the same

time the input should be challenging enough to sustain their motlv

it is too below their level, they may not learn much from it, and if it is too
above their level they will not be able to understand and interact with the

materials.
3. Benefits
텍 스 트 가 지 닌 진정《쭈운로 인 해 실 제 생 활 에 서 쓰 이 는 언 어 를 경 험 하 고 학 습 할 수

Through exploring 4656 materials, students have the opportunity 10 566

and hear 1631 language that serves 3 purpose. Another convincing reason to

use authentic samples is for their richness in cultural content. Because these

texts are prepared for native speakers, they reflect the details of everyday life

in a culture as well as its s@ql/‘value,

4, Drawbacks
진 정 성 있 는 텍 스 트 는 일 반 적 으 로 학 생 들 의 수 준 을 고 려 하 지 않 은 있 는 그 대 로 의

One of 06 challenges teachers often describe when using authentic texts 15
that these materials contain linguistic structures and vocabulary that students

may not nave already learned. Also, text is simply text, with varying levels of

sophistication or cémplexity, which can demotivate students.

2. Drawbacks

@ 모 든 내 용 이 학 습 자 의 요 구 에 적 합 한 것 은 아 니 며 , 상 당 한 양 의 보 충과 수 정 이

의 존 할 수 있 다 .

* Not all of the content corresponds to 116 needs of learners and it may

* Inexperienced teachers may rely too heavily on a textbook, following it in

(1) Adding
기 존 의 텍 스 트 에 양 혹 은 질 적 으 로 자 료 를 추 가 함 으 로 써 텍 스 트 를 보 완 한 다 .

Materials 316 supplemented by putting more into them. The teacher can do

this by either extending or expanding.

@ Extending : 같 은 학 습 내 용 , 같 은 수 준 의 연 습 활 동 등 을 추 가 함 으 로 써 양 적 으 로 만
자 료 를 추 가 하 는 ot (i)

The teacher supplies more of the 5306 type of material, thus making

a quantitative change 10 the material. This means that the techniques

are being applied within the methodological framework of the original

materials.

o Example 1

There is a practice which asks the learner to complete a sentence with the
missing verb in the correct form, such as the simple past. The coursebook
may have provided ten sentences for this treatment, but the teacher may
value this type of activity for her class and adapt the coursebook by
adding five more sentences with missing verbs.

o Example 2

The materials contain 3 practice 10 6 pronunciation of minimal pairs
(rice/lice, rake/lake) but not enough examples of the difficulties for
learners with 3 particular L1. Thus, the teacher put more examples with r-1

minimal pairs.

@ Expanding : 기 존 의 자 료 와 는 다 른 기 능 룬 연 습 활 동 , 다 른 차 원 의 언어
접 근 법 을 적 용 한 연 습 활 동 을 추가함。로써 텍 스 트 를 질 적 으 로 추 가 하 는 것 을
의 미 한 다

Expanding adds something different to the materials; the change 15
_랭꼬흩쁘둘뜨론》숟칙 The expanding goes further than extending by putting in a

different language skill or a new component.

234 . 844040 영 어 교 육 론 1

## Page 235

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L‘
? Example 1

The only pronunciation practice in the materials is on individual sounds
and minimal pairs, however, this may be necessary but not sufficient.
Therefore, the teacher decides 10 add some work on sentence stress. /' 다 드 치 썬 )

LWW

Although the new grammar material is important and relevant, the addition
of a discussion section at the end of the unit will help to reinforce and
contextualize the linguistic items covered. / 24 른 Gl A2v;)
L difeer (g Sleil]
(2) Deleting
기 존 텍 스 트 에 서 불 필 요 한 부 분 을 생 략 하 는 방 법 이 다 . ^40108 과 마 찬 가 지 로 양 적 인
방 법 과 질 적 인 방 법 이 있 다 .
Deletion is 06 opposite process to that of addition by omitting materials

from the original.

@ Subtracting : 동 일 한 언 어 기 능 내 에 서 지 나 치 게 많 은 연 습 활 농 을 생 략 하 는 등

햐처 으 로 텍 스 트 를 줄 이는 AL 의 미 한 다 .
Subtracting refers to reducing 416 161841 of materials 10 quantitative

way. It does not have a significant impact on the overall methodology.

o Example 1

A teacher decides to do five of the questions practising the simple past

tense instead of the ten in the coursebook.

? Example 2

Although a communicative coursebook has been selected as relevant in
our situation, some of the language functions are unlikely to be required
by learners. Such functions as “giving directions” or “greeting” may be
useful; “expressing sympathy” or “ordering things” may not. Thus the
teacher decided to skip the activities for latter functions.

O Example 1

O Example 2

made more meaningful if it is turned into an interactive exercise where all

students participate.

## Page 251

01. Complete the summary by filling in each blank with TWO to SIX words
from the passage. Change the word forms if necessary. [ 기 입 형 ]

Conventional wisdom in language teaching suggests that there is no

such thing as a perfect textbook. This is likely to be true whether the
materials in question were commercially produced or locally created
within a given program. Moreover, the task of completely reinventing
the materials for all the courses in a program on a continuous basis is a
staggering undertaking. One viable solution to both these problems is
to use what is of value in an existing set of materials, while adapting it
to the needs or changing needs of the program.
The first stage in adapting the textbook is to find out whether it serves
the students’ needs and helps meet the course objectives. In so doing,
it is important to identify the gaps that cannot be filled by the textbook.
Filling the gaps is adapting the textbook. What should the gaps be filled
with? Certainly, teachers in the program may know from their own
experience existing materials that may help to fill the gaps. Or materials
creation may be necessary for some of the missing elements.

Materials adaptation is regarded as a teacher’s attempt to (1) -
in the textbook by means of commercially available materials or materials
creation. It is usually done because it is extremely difficult to find a perfect
textbook or to (2) regularly.

Your answer 0

@

Chapter 07 교 재 개 발 및 교 재 분 석 251

## Page 252

Chapter

07 고 재 개 발 및 교 재 분 석

02. What is one potential problem 아 using authentic materials mentioned 10
the passage? Use Seven 10 Ten words. [ 기 입 형 ]

Using authentic materials is one of the mainstays of an imaginative
and motivating higher level course, but rarely features at levels lower
than intermediate. There are several reasons for this. One is a kind
of fear that students will panic when faced with language that is
largely unfamiliar. There is a feeling that, to prevent students from
panicking, the language should be edited to the students’ level. This is
an unnecessary fear, as using authentic materials can be rewarding and
stimulating for both teachers and students.

Authentic materials are linguistically difficult, but that’s not the
point. It is true that authentic materials, written or recorded, are likely
to be too hard even for advanced students. The trick, regardless of the
materials used, is not to edit and grade the materials, but to develop
and use 3 variety of tasks that suit your students’ abilities. Especially
with lower levels, it needs to be emphasized that students don’t have
to understand everything in order to perform the set tasks. Students
often don’t believe you until you go through 2 few tasks with them. By
helping students “conquer” authentic materials with a variety of tasks,
you come to build their confidence in coping with the unknown, an
important element in their development as independent learners.

Your answer

252 . 84104 영 어 교 육 론

## Page 253

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L—

03. Complete the summary by filling in the blank with. [ 기 입 형 ]

ESL/EFL teachers have access to a great number of authentic materials
and media. Using authentic materials and media has disadvantages
and advantages. One disadvantage is that it takes time and effort to
locate authentic materials. A second disadvantage is that it is sometimes
difficult to make authentic materials and media comprehensible to the
students. A third disadvantage is that some students will not accept
authentic materials and media as being a valuable learning source.

Although using authentic materials has disadvantages, there are very
strong reasons to use them. They can reinforce for students a direct
relation between the language classroom and the outside world. In
addition, authentic materials and media offer a way to contextualize

language learning.

Although authentic materials have disadvantages; they need (1) and
&) , is hard to make them (3) , they have strong advantages;
they give student (4) and (5) | what students learn.

06. Read the following and fill in the blank. [ 기 입 형 ]

The teacher encourages students to choose one story among graded

He was an inch, perhaps two, under six feet, powerfully built, and
he advanced straight at you with 3 light stoop 아 the shoulders,
head forward, and a fixed from-under stare which made you think

displayed a kind of dogged self-assertion.

He was an inch, perhaps two, under six feet, powerfully built,

(2) Maximize individual work
학 생 개 인 의 속 도와 능 력 에 맞 춰 맞 춤 과 제 를 제 공 하 도록 한 다 .

Perhaps we can get students to 456 graded readers 35 part of their
individual reading programme. When we get students to build their own
portfolio of work, we are asking them to work as individuals, too. We can
encourage students to make full use of a school library or self-access centre.
We can get students to write individually — offering their own responses to
what they read and hear. We can direct them to language learning websites,

or we can get them to produce their own blogs.

(3) 펀숀숀 student§

For example, we can appoint class monitors whose job it is to collect
homework or hand out worksheets. Students can take the register or organize
their classmates into groups. We can ask some of our students to teach the
others. This might mean asking individuals to be in charge of a group who
are p;eparing arguments for a debate for example, or who are going through
a worksheet. It might mean telling individual students that it is their job to
explain some language to their group. We need to choose our student ‘leaders’
with care, and we will then monitor their performance very carefully. This
will not only be useful for us, but may give them some satisfaction, too, and

this may affect their motivation very positively.

(4) Use 3 different pace for different activities

student to think about something, we will want to slow the pace right down.

@Aanaging Mixed Level Class
대 집 단 수 업 의 가 장 큰 문 제 점 중 의 하 나 가 학 생 들 의 언어 능 력 에 차 이 가 난 다 는

Many teachers 566 mixed level classes 35 especially problematic. Yet in a
real sense all classes have students with a mixture of different abilities and
language levels. Within the school environments, students are often streamed
— that is re-grouped for language lessons according to their abilities. In other
situations, however, such placement and streaming is not possible and so
teachers are faced with individuals who have different language knowledge,

different intelligences, different learning speeds, and different learning
styles and preferences. There is particular concern for the needs not only of
students who are having difficulty at the lower end of the scale, but also for

_glfted” students

For example, we might give student A a text form English language

newspaper about a certain topic. Student B might be directed to a website on

such as way 35 to 66 comprehensible to them. 10 this way, all the student are

working at their own individual levels.

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ .'1

@ Different levels

One way of offering different content is to allow students to make
choices about what material they are going to work with. For example,
we can offer them a range of possible grammar or vocabulary exercises
and they can choose which ones they want to do. If we wish them to read
outside the class, we will encourage them to choose which books they
want to read, since when they make their choice — rather than having
books chose for them — they are likely to read with enthusiasm.

® Drawbacks in 8 large class

Giving students different content is an ideal way to differentiate between
them. Nevertheless, it is extremely problematic in large classes. Not only
does it involve considerably more teacher preparation time than non-
differentiated content have to search out a range of different exercise and
materials for different individuals, but giving feedback to students in class
becomes a lot more complicated when we are responding to a number of
different tasks than it is when we are giving feedback about one.

For example, group A might have to interpret the information in the

on the other hand, might answer a series of open-ended questions. Group C
— the group we perceive 35 having the greatest need of support — might
be offered a series of multiple-choice questions; their task is to pick the
correct response from two or more alternatives because we think this will
be easier for them than having to interpret all the information themselves.

is 12. The more proficient students have a clear but high target to aim

for, but everyone, including those who are not so able, have something

_purposeful 10 do.

Students who are experiencing difficulty may need us to help them clear
up some problems; we might have to correct some language use, or help
them to organize information logically, for example. If they are working
on a webquest on the Internet, we might have to show them which link
to follow or what to do next. This kind of flexible response is one of the
main aspects of differentiation.

Being inclusive : mixed level class®| 가 장 큰 문 제 점 중 하 나는 일 부 학 생 들 이
수 업 에 뒤 쳐 져 있 거 나 , 참 여 하 지 않 는 다 는 것 이 다 .

If we spend a lot of 1016 with 016 higher-levels students in 3 class,
the students who are less linguistically able may feel that they are being
ignored and become demotivated 35 3 result. If, on the other hand, we
spend all our time with students who we think need our help more than
others, the higher-level students may feel neglected and unchallenged.
Such students quickly lose interest in the class and develop an attitude
which makes them difficult to work with.

® 141860 level class 교 사 들 은 모 든 학 생 들 이 수 업 에 참 여 할 수 있 도록 유 도 해 야 한 다 .

When setting 3 task with the whole 81040 (perhaps by asking initial
questions to build up a situation), teachers will want to start by working at
a level that all of the students are comfortable with. She will ask questions
that all the students can understand and relate to so that their interest is
aroused and so that they all the students can understand the goal they are
aiming for. Once they are all involved with the topic or the task, she may

allow for differentiation in any of the ways. But her initial task is include

soon start to behave as if they are excluded!

or

* The advanced level students pear to 66 more challenged 표 working with

@ Advantages 10 cross-ability. groupings

The input is likely to be comprehensible to the beginner.

* Matching work : Task A gives all the answers on the page for support.
They are jumbled for challenge. Weaker students manipulate the given
material, and can use logic to help match the task items, together with the
information in the reading text.

* Multiple choice questions : Task B gives multiple-choice answers 10 help
the average students. This is slightly different from the conventional “one
answer only is correct” multiple choice, since in questions 3 and 4 there is
more than one correct answer.

* Open questions : Task C gives open questions — with no extra support — to
challenge the stronger students in the group.

Task A: For Weaker Students

1. How many of the young people are 13 years old? (Three...)
2. How many boys are there?

3. Who doesn’t eat meat?

4. Who likes football?

5. Who lives in the country?

Task B: For Stronger Students

Write questions for these answers, based on the Penpal Page.

1. How many of them are 13? Three of them are

2. ? There are four.
3 ? Eloise doesn’t.
4. ? James does.
5 ? Chris does.

270 Build-up 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 271

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @ L—

With task A, weaker students answer questions about the text. With Task
B, stronger students write questions for given answers related to the text.
Because the answers to these two tasks are complementary, it would not
be an efficient use of class time for the teacher to conduct postactivity
feedback with the whole class. Instead, student-students feedback would be
a good idea, with the students in AB pairs. The teacher’ should naturally be
available as an arbiter if there are any questions of their own. If there are
grammatically correct, and fit the given answers, the teacher should confirm
them as also correct. This type of feedback, in weak/strong pairs, is very
motivating for the weaker students. They have got the difficult questions that
the strong students have struggled 10 reconstruct. For weak students, already .
knowing key information is 3 pleasant change from traditional whole-class -
oral feedback, which often turns into a dialogue between the teacher and the

brightest and most forthcoming students.

0 Example 2

pair, where the former and the latter take the role of the receiver and

sender roles respectively.

. The teacher tells students 10 each pair that the two maps 316 similar,

of your maps are different.

partner, the higher proficient, a route. The task is completed when

the student who know the route successfully communicates it to the
students who doesn’t know it.

Glossary

Acculturation 문 화 변 용

The process by which a person integrates 1010 3 particular culture. Success in
second language learning 15 related to the learner’s degree of acculturation
into the second language culture. Acculturation hypothesis was one of the first
theories of SLA that attempted to prioritize social factors over purely cognitive

ones.

문 화 변 용 이란 개 인이 특 정 문 화 속 으 로 통 합 되 어 가 는 과 정 을 뜻 하 며 , ' 흥 분 , 기 뽑 ㅡ culture shock — culture
stress - ㅡ * 1600\8『/' 의 4 단 계 를 거 치 게 된 다 . 문 화 변 용 가 설 은 언 어 , 사 고 , 문 화 사 이 에 는 밀 접 한 관 련이 있 다 는
Sapir-Whort hypothesisOil Al 출 발 하 였 으 며 , 문 화 변 용 의 정 도 는 제 2 언 어 습 득 의 성 공 과 밀 접 한 관 련이 있 다 .

Accuracy 정 확 성

The extent to which 3 learner’s 456 of a second language conforms to the 10165
of the language. It is usually measured in terms of grammatical accuracy but
it is also possible to talk about accuracy of vocabulary and pronunciation as
well. Accuracy is often contrasted with fluency, which means the capacity to
be communicative in realtime conditions. Accuracy was once thought to be a
precondition for fluency. But to withhold fluency activities until the learners are
accurate is now considered unrealistic. Research into 6 order of acquisition of

grammatical structures suggests that accuracy may be “late-acquired”.

정 확 성 이란 제 2 언 어 의 2, 어 휘 , 발 음 등 의 규 칙 체 계 와 학 습 자 가 사 용 한 언 어 의 일 치 정 도 를 의 미 한 다 .
CLT 이 전 관 점 에 서는 정 확 성 을 유 창 성 (14600>/) 과 대 립 되 는 개 념 으 로 , 유 창 성 을 위 한 필 수 조 건 으 로 간 주 하 여 ,
정 확 하 게 언 어 를 사 용 할 때 까 지 유 창 성 을 보 류 하 였 다 . 그 러 나 현 재 0 관 점 에 서는 정 확 성과 유 창 성 을 상호
보 완 적 인 관 계 로 B3, accuracy®} fluency 사 이 의 균 형 을 강 조 한 다 .

Age and Second language learning 나 이 와 제 2 언 어 학 습 과 의 관 계

The precise relationship between age and 560010 language learning remains
unresolved and controversial. There are cognitive and maturity differences
between children and adults that make a difference in language learning. The
rate of acquisition and ultimate level of success varies. However, with regard
to grammar, research suggests that the acquisition process is largely unaffected
by age. That is, both children and adults go through the same stages (order of

acquisition).

제 2 언 어 학 습 에 있 어 서 어 느 연 령 이 가 장 좋 은 지 , 언어 학 습 이 언 제 시 작 되 는 지 아 직 명 확 하 게 밝 혀 진 것 은 없 디
은 아 동 과 성인 학 습 자 간 에 는 언어 학 습 에 영 향 을 끼 치 는 차 이 가 존 재 한 다 는 것 이 다 . 즉 , 언어 습 득
속 도와 학 습 도 달 점 ( 성 공 의 정 도 ) 은 아 동 과 성인 학 습 자 에 있 어 서 차 이 가 존 재 한 다 . 하 지만 아 동 과 성 인 의

속
습 득 은 연 령 에 영 향 을 받 지 않 고 동 일 한 과 정 을 보 인 다 .

Attention 주 의 짐 중

표 you pay attention 10 something, you direct mental effort at it, while ignoring
other things. The notion that learning requires attention has led to the established
view that it is necessary for language learner to notice the form taken by an
utterance in order to acquire the grammar that it contains. However, we are

capable 아 learning some aspects of language implicitly.

주 의 집 중 의 정 도 는 학 습 에 영 향 을 끼 치 는 528 요 소 이 다 . 하 지만 최 근 의 관 점 에 서는 언어 2400 대 한

Audiolingual method, Audiolingualism 구두 청 각 교 수 법

The audiolingual method was derived from 3 view of language that was based
around sentence structure and learning that emphasized habit-formation. Its most
distinctive feature was the drilling of sentence patterns. As in the direct method,
spoken language was prioritized; translation and the use of meta-language were
discouraged; and accuracy was considered to be a precondition for fluency.

Authenticity 진 정 성

A classroom text is authentic if it was written for a non-classroom audience such
as a newspaper article or a pop song, whereas a course book drill or dialogue are
not. In order to make these upgraded texts accessible to learners, an approach
based on the principle “grade the task, not the text” was developed.

Communicative approach?| 등 장 으 로 진 정 성이 떨 어 지는 texti= 실 생 활 의 읽 기 , 듣 기를 위 해 서는 부 적 당 한

Automaticity 자 동 성

If you can perform 3 task automatically, you can do it without having to 120005
attention on it. In language speaking terms, this automatization process means
being able to draw on a set of internalized procedures in order to construct

spoken utterances under the pressure of time.

내 재 화 된 과 정 에 따 라 의 식 적 인 집 중 없 이 특 정 과 업 , 학 습 을 수 행 해 나 가 는 것 을 의 미 한 다 . 말 하 기 과 업 에 서

Autonomy 자 용 성

Autonomy is your capacity 10 take responsibility for, and control of your own
learning. It is also called self-directed learning. Autonomous learning assumes
that the learner has well-developed learning strategies and the development of

such strategies is the aim of learner training.

자 율 성은 학 습 자 AAR 학 습 을 조 절 하 는 능 력 을 뜻 한 다 . 학 습 자 나 름 의 학 습 전 략 은 자 율 성 을 높 이는 데 중 요 한
요 소 이 며 , 학 생 들 에 게 과 업 활 동 의 선 택 권 을 부 여 하 거 나 개 별 화 학 습 을 제 공 하 는 것 은 학 습 자 의 자 율 성 을 높 이는

Behaviorism, Behaviorist learning theory 항 동 주 의

Behaviorism is the psychological theory that viewed learning as a form of
habit formation. According to behaviorism, habits are formed when the
learner’s responses to external stimuli are positively reinforced. To behaviorist
psychologists, language was just another kind of behavior, and it could be
conditioned. The language teaching method that is associated with behaviorism is
called audiolingualism. 10 this method, 6 stimulus-response-reinforce cycle was
realized through pattern practice drills. Use of the learners’ L1 was discouraged,
since it might lead to bad habits. Errors were avoided for the same reason.

행 동 주 의 에 서는 학 습 을 습 관 의 형 성 이라 보 고 반 복 , 모 방 , 훈련 등 을 통 한 학 습 을 중 시 한 다 . 따 라 서 올 바 른 언
사 용 을 위해 모 국 어 의 사 용 은 간 섭 이라 간 주 하 여 모 국 어 의 사 용 은 가 급 적 하 지 않 으 며 , 구두 청 각 교 수 법 의
발 달 에 영 향 을 끼 쳤 다 . 행 동 주 의 학 습 이 론 은 한 번 도 강 화 를 받 지 못 한 언 어 적 행 동 도 관 찰 된 다 는 비 판 을 받 게
된 다 . 따 라 서 학 습 자 는 자 신 의 언어 체 계 를 창 조 적 으 로 발 달 시 킨 다 는 생 득 주 의 , 인 지 주 의 로 이 어 지 게 된 다 .

Bilingualism 이 중 언 어 사 용

Bilingualism is the use of two languages. Bilingual users of English speak English
as their second language alongside one or more other languages. According
to behaviorist learning theory, the first language interfered with the second.
Accordingly, the two languages were kept separate. However, 3 distinction 15
made between additive bilingualism, in which the second language is added to
the first without threatening the speaker’s first language identity, and subtractive
bilingualism, 10 which the second language replaces the first, thereby threatening

the speaker’s first language identity.

이중 언어 사 용 (2 개 언어 상 용 ) 은 2 가 지 의 다 른 언 어 를 사 용 하 는 것 을 의 미 하 지 만 , 2 개 의 언 어 를 똑 같 이 우 수 하 게
사 용 하 는 것 만 을 의 미 하 지 않 는 다 . 이중 언어 사 용 은 20006 bilingualism} subtractive 60943150 으 로 LFFICt,
전 자 는 모 국 어 에 대 한 언어 정 체 성에 혼 란 없 이 목 표 어 를 사 용 하 는 것 을 의 미 하 며 , 후 자 는 제 2 언 어 가 모 국 어 와
모 국 어 에 대 한 정 체 성 을 대 체 하 는 경 우 를 가 리 킨 다 .

Classroom interaction 고 실 에 서 의 상 호 작 용

Classroom interaction is the general term for what goes on between the people

10 the classroom, particularly when it involves language. 10 traditional classrooms,

Appendix 01 Glossary 285

## Page 286

Appendix

—J I:il Glossary

most interaction 15 initiated by the teacher, and learners either respond
individually, or in unison. In order to increase the amount of student involvement
and interaction, teacher—learner interaction is often combined with pair-work and
group-work. These are associated with a more learner-centered approach.

전 통 적 인 교 수 법 에 서 의 상 호 작 용 은 주 로 교 사 중 심 으 로 이 루 어 졌 으 나 현 재 의 관 점 에 서는 학 생 들 간 의 상 호 작 용 이

ㅇ 090714\6 learning theory 인 지 주 의 학 습 이론

According to the cognitive psychologist, language develops out of the child’s
thoughts and growing awareness of the world. This is at variance with the
view that language is “hard-wired” in the brain, and that the capacity to learn
languages is something we are born with. A later version of cognitive learning
theory suggests that the child acquires language by forming and testing
-hypotheses about the adult language it hears around it.

Coherence sz

If a text is coherent, it makes sense. The coherent sentences 316 related to each
other, and relate in our background knowledge of the world. The following
text is coherent: We want you to be delighted with this facility. If there is a fault
with the toilet please call extn 1071 on the nearest white courtesy phone. The
language and organization of the text are appropriate, especially when we know
that the text was written to be read in an airport restroom. Moreover, the two

sentences of the text have a logical relation.

Cohesion 걸 함 력

If a text is cohesive, 15 elements 2316 connected. Cohesion is the 1456 of
grammatical and lexical means to achieve connected text, either spoken or
written. Unlike coherence, which different readers or listeners may experience to
varying degrees, cohesion is a stable property of text. And while cohesion may

help make a text coherent, it cannot guarantee it.

Collocation =i

If two words collocate, they frequently occur together. The relation between the
words may 66 grammatical, such 35 verb-particular prepositions (i.e. depend on)
and verb-nouns (i.e. make an arrangement). The collocation may also 66 lexical

as when two content words regularly co-occur.

Communication strategy 의 사 소 통 전 략

Communication strategies 316 ways that learners get round the fact that they may
not know how to say something. Most communication strategies are directed
at filling in 016 gaps 10 the learner’s vocabulary knowledge. If they help the
speakers achieve their intended message, they are labeled achievement strategies.
But the speaker might decide that the message is simply not achievable. Then
they might adopt what is called an avoidance strategy.

Communicative approach 의 사 소 톡 적 점 근 범

The communicative approach, 3150 known as communicative language teaching
(CLT), is 30 umbrella term used to describe 3 major shift in emphasis 10 language
teaching. The shift was away from teaching language systems such as vocabulary
and grammar in isolation to teaching people how these systems are used in
real communication; and from viewing linguistic competence alone as being
the goal of language learning to replacing it with the notion of communicative
competence. This entailed a major re-think of syllabus design, materials,
classroom practice and testing. Thus, communicative terms supplemented, or
replaced, the traditional grammar syllabus with the functional-notional syllabus.

언 어 사 용 의 목 적 을 기 능 , 의 사 소 통에 보 다 초 점 을 두 고 학 습 자 들 의 상 호 작 용 을 중 시 하 여 등 장 한 의 사 소 통 적
접 근 법 은 언어 학 습 에 중 심 을 기 존 의 언 어 의 개 별 적 인 문 법 적 요 소 에 서 의 사 소 통 능 력 으 로 의 관 점 의 변 화 를
가 져 왔 다 . 또 한 문 법 요 소 들 로 T4 structure—based 5\/13645 에 서 notional—functional syllabus2 변 화 했 다 .

가 져 왔 다 .

Communicative competence 의 사 소 통 능 력

Communicative competence 15 what you know 10 order to 66 able to
communicate effectively. The term contrasts with competence (now known as
linguistic competence), which is the knowledge that enables the creation 아 well-
formed sentences. The conception of competence suggested the need to re-
define the goals of language teaching, and to design a methodology to match.
Thus, the notion of communicative competence fuelled the development of the

communicative approach.

Community Language Learning (CLL) 38#i 인어 교 수 범

Also called counseling learning, CLL is a teaching method that falls within the
humanistic tradition. The method places the learners 31 center stage, allowing
them the responsibility of deciding the content of the lesson. The teacher adopts

a largely consultant role.

Comprehension questions 이 해 확 인 을 위 한 질 문

Comprehension questions 316 often used 10 conjunction with reading or listening
texts. In theory, the purpose of comprehension questions is to check learners’
understanding of a text. An alternative, more helpful approach, is to set questions
in advance of listening or reading, and which learners answer while reading or
listening. These can act to focus learners’ attention during the task, and can be
staged from 101031 gist-checking questions 10 questions requiring more intensive

processing of the text.

Concordance gof 42!

A concordance is 3 list of words 10 3 text, or in 3 corpus along with each word’s
immediate context. It functions like an index and is a convenient way in which
corpus information can be made available for study. Concordances can show,
for example, the typical collocations of selected words. Concordances used to be

compiled by hand, but now software programs can process text rapidly.

Conjunction &t

Conjunctions are members of a word class whose function is to join together
words, phrases, clauses and sentences. There are two types of conjunction: co-
ordinating and subordinating. Co-ordination conjunction, such as “and” and “but”
join equivalent units. Subordinating conjunctions such 35 “if”, “because” and

“when” join 3 subordinate clause to a main clause.

Connectionism 2z

Connectionism is 3 way of using computers 10 model the way that the brain
processes and learns information, including language. Connectionist models are
based upon the way that the brain’s neural networks function by being massively
interconnected. Overtime, the connections in the network are strengthened
or weakened according to how frequently they are used, and according to
whether the output from the system is correct or not. In this way, connections
are established and the network “learns”: strong, weak, zero and negative

associations are built up in the form of connection strengths.

연 결 주 의 는 뇌 의 정 보 처 리 과 정 으 로 언어 학 습 을 설 명 하 고 자 한 다 . 즉 뇌 의 세 포 들 이 새 로 운 정 보 에 대 해 서 로
연 결 된 망 을 형 성 하 여 정 보 를 받 아 들 이는 것 처 럼 언어 학 습 도 노 출 된 어 휘 , 문 장 들 간 의 연 결 을 형 성 하 여 언 어 를
학 습 하 게 된 다 는 것 이 다 . 연 결 의 빈 도 수 가 높 은 문 구 나 문 장 은 강 한 네 트 워 크 를 형 성 하 여 뇌 에 저 장 이 되 고 연 결

Consciousness-raising (CR) 24 82

Consciousness-raising describes the way that learners become aware, or are
made aware, of features of the language they are learning. The term belongs

290 Buildup 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 291

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers ® L—

to cognitive learning theory, which claims a central role for conscious mental
operations in learning. At the very least, it is argued learners need to notice
features of the input, if these features are to become intake. Of course, teachers
can highlight input features but this is no guarantee that learners will notice them.
So it may be better to talk about the things that have CR potential. These include:
enhancing the input to make certain items more salient; inductive learning;

noticing the gap; and pushed output.

Constructivism 구 서 주 의

Constructivism is 3 theory of learning that claims that individuals actively
construct knowledge, rather than passively receiving it. They do this by filtering
and organizing their experience so as to match their existing knowledge of the
world. If there is a mismatch between their experience and their existing mental
representations, they restructure the latter to accommodate the new information.
According to this view, different individuals who are exposed to the same
experiences will each construct a different and unique reality. The view of the
learner as an individual, actively constructing personal meaning, has obvious
implication for language teaching. For a start, it suggests such passive learning
procedures as imitation and rote learning may play only a minor role. It supports
the case for learner-centered instruction, and for learners discovering how the
language works for themselves. Finally, since learning is the construction of
personal meaning constructivism underscore the argument for personalization.
A form of constructivism which emphasizes the dynamic interplay between
individuals and their social context is called social constructivism, and shares

many of the principles of socio-cultural learning theory.

성 주 의 학 습 이 론 에 따 르 면 학 습 자 는 제 공 되 는 지 식 을 받 아 들 이는 수 동 적 인 존 재 가 아 니 라 스 스 로 지 식 을
구 성 하 는 존 재 이 다 . 자 신 만 의 인 지 와 경 험 등 을 사 용 하 여 같 은 지 식 이라 할 지 라 도 개 개 의 학 습 자 는 지 식 의 형 태 ,
의 미 는 다 르 게 형 성 하 며 때 로는 새 로 운 지 식 을 창 출 하 기 도 한 다 . 따 라 서 학 습 의 주 체 는 학 습 자 가 되 어 야 하 며 ,

Content and Language Integrated Learning (CLIL) ug-otof 52 &t

Teaching a subject through English, to learners whose first language is not English
is known as content and language integrated learning (content-based teaching).
CLIL belongs firmly in the tradition of the strong form of the communicative
approach, in that there is no predetermined language syllabus. Instead,
instruction is organized solely around the content. It is also closely related to
immersion teaching, whereby learners in a situation of additive bilingualism take
all or some of their school subjects in a second language. Content and language
integrated learning instruction probably works best where teachers 316 adept not
only at teaching the subject matter, but also at addressing their learners’ language

needs.

내 용 - 언 어 통 합 학 습 은 내 용 교 과 를 영 어로 가 르 치 는 내 용 기반 교 수로 의 사 소 통 적 접 근 법 의 1009 versionol

Context 2

The context of a language item is its adjacent language items. In the absence
of context, it is often impossible to assign exact meaning to an item. A word
or sentence in one context can have a very different meaning in another. It is
sometimes necessary to distinguish between different kinds of context. On the
one hand, there is the context of the accompanying text, sometimes called the
co-text. Both co-text and context influence the production and interpretation of

language.

문 맥 이란 인 접 한 어 휘 항 목 들 간 의 연 결 로 인 해 형 성 되 는 맥 락 을 의 미 한 다 . 글 을 이 루 는 큰 흐 름 이라 2 수 있 는 데
하 나 의 문 맥 에 U= 어 휘 는 다 른 문 맥 에 서는 다 른 의 미 로 사 용 될 수 도 있 다 . 00066※ 는 word level, sentence level
이 상 에 서 형 성이 되 기 때 문에 담 화 분 석 에 서 이 러 한 text 내 에 서 의 의 미 혹 은 |6* 들 간 의 의 미 를 분 석 하 게 된 다 .

292 . 8400-40 영 어 교 육 론 Ｌ

## Page 293

Guideline for Pre-service Teachers @

Contrastive analysis 대 조 분 석 가 설

Contrastive analysis refers to the way that the linguistic systems of two languages
are compared and contrasted. A comparison between a learner’s first language
and a second language would predict the errors that a learner would make
learning the latter: the greater the difference, the more likely an error. The
underlying assumption was the behaviorist one that errors occur as a result of
interference when the learner transfers first language habits into the second.
10 fact, error analyses of learners’ inter-language show that many errors that
might be predicted on the basis of contrastive analysis do not occur. Others that
are not predicted occur. For these reasons, many errors are now attributed to
developmental causes. That is they occur as a result of the way inter-language
develops naturally, irrespective of the learner’s first language. CA’s most
successful predictions 316 10 the 3163 of phonology where the influence of the

first language is more in evidence than it is in grammar learning.

행 동 주 의 의 영 향 으 로 발 달 한 대 조 분 석 은 모 국 어 의 간 섭 을 오 류 의 원 인 으 로 보 고 모 국 어 와 목 표 어 의 대 조 를
